1. Excellence
1.1 Objectives  
Plastics bring unprecedented value in terms of convenience, versatility of design and lightweight to European consumers as well as increasingly advanced performances even in high end applications. Independently of the duration of use of the plastic goods, they all share insufficient recycling rate and, while the limit of our natural resources has been identified as one of our key societal challenges decades ago, the depletion continues. Only 31% of plastic packaging are currently recycled and an excessive 30-40% are landfilled, this being due to infra-developed technologies or to their unsatisfactory economic viability1. This is in fact aggravated by considering plastics as commodity where their economic value is linked to a single use, often not taking into account the potentially generated end of life (EoL) hurdles. Plastics have ubiquitous uses but also too often uncontrolled leakage in the environment (according to the Ellen MacArthur Foundation2, as much as 32% end up out of controlled waste management systems) among others due to the low incentives for their secondary use. In line with the just released Plastic Strategy for Europe (as well as with the Packaging Waste Law to come into force e.g. in Germany in 2019 assigning the core responsibility to producers and introducing sanctions below threshold recyclability rates), the time has come to shift to a Circular Model in the plastic sector improving the recycling rate but also the value of secondary raw materials (SRM) from plastic recycling. As such, to support significant technological advancements to meet such strategy and with an overarching mission to maximise the valorisation of our finite plastic resources, MultiCycle will develop a tailored sustainable process approach to the recycling of thermoplastic-based multi-materials to enable their use in multiple life cycles at their outmost properties. Our process, which is applicable to virtually any plastic or composite material, will be demonstrated in 2 main large volume applications: 1. Multilayer packaging (including PET, PE, PP and barrier layers such as EVOH, PA, metallised films) but also flexible films that cannot be recycled cost effectively to date. This accounts for around 50% of plastic packaging, i.e. ca. 10 mio tons/year in EU. 2. Fibre reinforced thermoplastics (FRP) (e.g. based on PA12 or PP matrix, long glass or carbon fibres). Plastic parts constitute around 16% of EU End-of-Life Vehicles (ELVs) wastes, i.e. ca. 1 mio tons/y (details of market size in section 2.2.2.F)15. On the one hand, while nowadays multilayer plastic films are needed to pack gas sensitive goods and their share on the packaging market is increasing (in some cases flexible barrier solutions even substitute rigid packaging), they are not recycled in practice, since pure polymers are needed for reprocessing, and the vast majority of them end up in landfill or energy recovery systems. In fact, even small monolayer films as used for pouches, lids, etc. are difficult to handle in the context of current mechanical recycling plants and only rarely recycled. Furthermore, despite their high value and sustainability potential, there has been very little evolution so far when it comes to materials recycling of bioplastics, such as PLA3, due to insufficient market penetration and/or economic levers. On the other hand, there is an increasing use of fibre reinforced plastic parts in the transport industry especially for lightweight purposes. Nevertheless, the mechanical recycling of composites, even based on thermoplastic matrices that can be melted and are increasingly used instead of thermosetting counterparts due to sustainability and process advantages, poses several limitations like high energy consumption, partial polymer degradation and downgrading the fibre length due to the grinding needed before reprocessing. Therefore, recovered materials are often directed to low value applications or only a minor recycled material fraction can be mixed with virgin materials to avoid deteriorating parts properties. Similarly to packaging, bioplastics (e.g. bio-PA11, bio-PA10,10, bio-PA4,10, PA5,6) are emerging in the automotive sector as matrix material in composites and require to define a cost-competitive recycling route. For both previous applications, we will use a novel process (CreaSolv®4) that has been patented by partner FRAUNHOFER 
 
1 https://ec.europa.eu/commission/news/first-ever-europe-wide-strategy-plastics-2018-jan-16_en 2 The new plastics economy rethinking the future of plastics, Ellen MacArthur foundation, 2016 3 PLA presents limited organic recyclability at high thickness or when laminated with certain materials. Other selected bioplastics, bio-PA, PET, PE are durable. 4 CreaSolv® is a registered trademark of the Creacycle GmbH, Grevenbroich, Germany 
 Fig 1: MutiCycle recycling process allowing multiple multimaterials’ 
life cycles in automotive and packaging value chains 

                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 10 of 117 
and is currently being industrially scaled up for polyolefin (PO)-based packaging applications. This is a solvent based selective extraction process that allows pure plastics to be recovered due to their specific solubility. It has no by-product (solvents can be used in a closed loop endlessly with only few ppm lost in each cycle) and a very low environmental impact demonstrated by peer-reviewed LCAs. Unlike general chemical recycling that generates monomers requiring a new polymerization step, CreaSolv® generates polymers. One further advantage is that by using successive steps with tailored solvents, even mixed input materials can be recycled. Furthermore, due to the mild conditions used, it maintains the polymer properties. For composites, CreaSolv® showed to preserve the quality and length of the reinforcement fibres. Since it allows removing contaminants and possibly revalorizing additives, it also solves a significant challenge for mechanical recycling of complex primary formulations when trying to match the composition required for the secondary application.  MultiCycle’s goal is to build on past promising results, overcome the remaining challenges of their demonstration, and prepare for their market uptake. As a preamble to our MultiCycle recycling process chain, we will deliver tailored solutions to identify/monitor the composition of input mixed multi-materials. Such waste will therefore fuel the novel separation process allowing the recovery of the single polymer fractions with properties equivalent to virgin counterparts and long reinforcement fibres. The process for recycling packaging and automotive materials will be upscaled in an industrial pilot plant that will be built and will include a smart control system based on Process Analytical Technology (PAT). In terms of subsequent reprocessing, any adjustment in performance for the secondary use will be handled by optimising the compound formulation. In order to facilitate wide market adoption, the characterisation of reprocessed materials and accessible applications thereof will be given utmost attention to match specified requirements for their secondary use (e.g. packaging for food & personal care applications and textiles-based reinforced & composites automotive parts). The potential to substitute a high amount of virgin materials will be a key pull strategy for the recycling value chain and for reducing the overall carbon footprint of the plastics industry along multiple life cycles. As the recycling of traditionally non-recyclable materials depends not only on the availability of suitable solutions, but also on economic viability considerations, the CAPEX and OPEX of the Multicycle process will be calculated and compared with other solutions whenever available. In addition, LCA and LCC (life cycle and life cycle costs assessments) will allow the demonstration of the most sustainable EoL approach. Based on the economic & environmental analysis, a decision support tool (DST) will be developed using different value chain scenarios. The assessment will recommend optimal network connections on the basis of multi-objective optimisation such as techno-economic criteria (cost, profit, efficiency), environmental and sustainability metrics and will support the creation of circular processing paths and materials’ use.  In parallel with upscaling activities leading to price and performance improvements, MultiCycle will include a number of outreach activities to improve both industry and society acceptance of recycled plastics. The project will deliver a set of recommendations for stakeholders and policymakers to overcome barriers such as reluctance to use market available recycled materials due to perceived property or variability issues, as well as the deficient regulations and standards. These recommendations will cover the benefits for the different stakeholders along the supply and value chain of the new recycled materials and will facilitate engagement and lobbying towards decision makers in the marketplace. To sustain the industrial initiatives investing in new recycling facilities, they will also provide suggestions for the creation of Tax and Incentive Schemes based on the percentage of recycle-able/ed resources to transition to materials with lower environmental burden when considered in a holistic circular use. Further outreach will centre on raising consumer perceptions in relation to responsible choice for more sustainable materials. To support the roll out of the project results in the future processing industries, the project will also cover technical and transversal training activities showing the new processes and valuable SRM’s applications. They will be a vector for preparing the next generation of EU recycling professionals and for cross-fertilization with other related projects, encapsulated in our dissemination activities including a Circular Plastic Helix creation. The consortium includes all stakeholders to prepare the MultiCycle supply and value chain from (bio)polymer producers and recyclers, converters and technological solution providers. Furthermore, end users are involved from the packaging and automotive industries as well as associations with demonstrated access to policy and decisions makers, innovation and training experts. While our project will focus on the needs of the above stated sectors, both in terms of post-consumer (pc) and post-industrial (pi) wastes, the relevance of its results goes far beyond, extending for example to the recycling of coated and/or glued materials, polymer blends (even mixed residues from polyolefins recycling plants), micro/nanoparticles reinforced composites, among others. InSPIREd by the Circular Economy ethos, the overriding goal of MultiCycle is to deliver an innovative recycling pilot plant and demonstrate that the resulting processes are economically & environmentally sustainable for valorising currently non-recyclable materials (multilayer packages and composites). The project will combine several approaches to optimise the material recycling: i) recycling and reprocessing upscaling in an industrial environment, ii) advanced monitoring and PAT solutions, iii) demonstration of recovered materials’ potential for packaging and automotive applications. By overcoming the challenges linked to multi-materials recycling, setting up the initial value chains, raising industry acceptance and demand for more sustainable materials, MultiCycle will have enduring impacts for significantly reduced landfill and environmental impact as well as increased consumer satisfaction.  To realise this, over a 36-month work plan, the following specific objectives, linked to their respective WPs and 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 11 of 117 
performance indicators (later used as milestones), are defined: Objective 1: To define the specifications of the materials to be recycled via the tailored MultiCycle processes (WP1) After an updated state of the art, patent and market review, a survey of available end of life options and the market requirements, the specifications of the packaging and fibre reinforced composites which will be recycled will be defined. This work will be conducted in synergy with the regulatory analysis performed in WP4-5-9. The process will also be mapped by the recycling plant leader and converters of the recovered materials (steps, throughputs, scales, etc.), as well as the strategy to maximise the overall economic/environmental viability of the MultiCycle recycling approach. Performance Indicator: By M4, the materials to be recycled, requirements of their recycling process, target properties of recovered materials to be used in packaging and automotive demonstrators, the analytical methods to prove them and benchmarks will be defined. Objective 2: To adjust the MultiCycle process to the selected materials in an existing pilot plant and to establish their recycling scenarios (WP2) Based on previous projects and partner’ patents, the recycling process will be tuned in an already existing small pilot (recycling 5-10kg of materials per day) by selecting the solvent composition and optimising the subsequent steps to maximise the recovery rate. Performance Indicators: By M12, the process to recover >80% of the polymers, fibres and other selected substances from packaging and FRC will be specified for its upscaling and recovered materials will be available in sufficient quantity to run reprocessing work at pilot scale (>20 kg of each fraction). Objective 3: To design a dedicated MultiCycle industrial pilot plant and develop the waste monitoring and PAT-based process control approach to optimise the upscaled recycling process (WP2, 4, 7) In line with the previous activities, a flexible industrial pilot plant capable of recycling both packaging and composites will be designed with particular attention to the performance of each step and to safety considerations. The most suitable photonics techniques for monitoring the composition of the waste to recycle will be evaluated and developed, and the process monitoring systems will also be established for following the evolution of the extraction steps and the purity of the recovered fractions. Performance Indicators: By M18, the MultiCycle pilot plant (equipped with a PAT control system) that can run at a yield of > 50 times the existing pilot plant will have been finely designed & engineered based on the results from objective 3 and the initial LCA /LCC results available by M14. Objective 4: To characterize the fibres recovered from the MultiCycle process (WP3) The long glass and carbon fibres (CF & GF) recovered will be characterized. Performance Indicators: By M15, the reusability of the recovered fibres will be defined especially in terms of mechanical properties and sizing agents. Objective 5: To develop the suitable compounds based on recovered additives and polymers (WP3) As the main target is to substitute virgin materials in packaging and automotive applications (including textile and composites), following the characterisation of the materials that have been recycled, recompounding trials will be performed. Recycling based on supercritical sc-CO2 will be used to remove any volatile organic compounds (VOCs) and to improve the properties and dispersion of the additives in the matrix. Performance Indicators: By M21, the reprocessing route for both conventional and bio-based polymers for packaging and automotive will be specified at pilot scale using at least 80% of recovered materials from the MultiCycle process, and the compound formulations suitable for film and fibres making will be selected for upscaling phase. Objective 6: To integrate the industrial pilot plant to upscale the recycling process (WP4) The different elements of the pilot plant, including the PAT monitoring systems, will be integrated at LOEMI. The process will be set up based on the results at small pilot scale and the safety and functionality thoroughly verified. Performance indicators: By M24, the tailored industrial pilot line able to recycle at least 25 kg of plastic packaging or FRP per hour will be operating and materials will start being delivered to WP5-6 (ca. 5 Tons expected till the end of WP4). Objective 7: To demonstrate the use of MultiCycle process for waste packaging and FRP at TRL7 (WP4) Large scale demonstration trials will be performed for each selected material type (fig 12 and 13). Performance indicators: By M27, the potential for recycling at least 80% of the currently not recycled packaging fractions will be established as well as the optimised route for recycling CFC and GFC demonstrating MultiCycle process at TRL7. Objective 8: To perform large scale converting trials of recovered materials into new packaging (WP5) The materials recovered from the MultiCycle pilot will be characterized and compared to virgin benchmarks for specified food (secondary packaging or food contact layer from pi-waste) and personal care packaging. Performance indicators: By M34, small series of prototype packaging demonstrators will be produced containing >70% of recycled materials (approx. 18 types of monolayer films, 6 coextruded films, 8 multi-layer laminates and end packaging format) and tested to confirm the usability of the materials recovered using MultiCycle process meeting applicable legislations and industrial requirements. Objective 9: To perform large scale trials regarding the conversion of recovered materials into new automotive parts (composites with short and long fibres, textiles) (WP6) The materials recovered from the MultiCycle pilot will be processed as injected parts (GF reinforced compounds) and as thermoformable nonwovens where both the fibres and polymers come from recycled streams. These will be characterized and compared to virgin benchmarks. In addition, the recovered thermoplastics will be processed to textile materials used in 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 12 of 117 
the car interior. Performance indicators: By M34, small series of prototype automotive parts containing >70% of recycled materials (including at least 6 types of demonstrators semi-finished and final components) will confirm the usability of the materials recovered using MultiCycle process meeting industrial requirements. Objective 10: To demonstrate the final processes and products’ environmental & economic sustainability (WP7) An LCA, S-LCA (societal) and LCC will be performed on the developed processes and resulting materials to show the reduced environmental impact vs. current end of life options. The overall techno-economic viability will be evaluated with inputs from all previous WPs. Performance indicators: By M34, the LCA, S-LCA, LCC, CAPEX, OPEX, techno-economic assessments will confirm the future commercial viability and improvement vs. current solutions. Objective 11: To develop a DST for the assessment of environmental and economic sustainability (WP7) A Decision Support Tool (DST) will be developed to determine the optimisation path for the reuse of the recycled materials based on the environmental, economic and social sustainability of the entire value-chain. Performance indicators: By M35, the user-friendly DST-based software will be up and running. Objective 12: To leverage training and capacity building activities for stakeholders along the value chains and to inform and influence policy and decisions makers towards an improved recycling framework (WP8) A set of measures will be undertaken to establish a positive framework to stimulate the uptake of the MultiCycle innovation. Targeted communication and training campaigns will be performed. Furthermore, we will intervene at macro level by lobbying towards relevant policy makers for the creation of initiatives that promote recycling and the use of recycled materials to impulse a paradigm shift towards more sustainable manufacturing and consumption. Performance indicator: By M6, our impactful Vision 2020 Circular Plastics Helix will be running to leverage significant mobilisation around the project (target 500 affiliates by the end of the project). By M36, a robust communication strategy will have ensured the recognition of MultiCycle as a reference recycling process in the plastic sector. At least 500 process engineering professionals and 500 students will have been trained regarding MultiCycle. Position papers, policy and regulatory recommendations will have been addressed to the EC among other relevant regulatory bodies.   Objective 13: To maximise the innovation impacts of MultiCycle, including growth and jobs creation, via post project industrial commercialisation of the project outcome (WP9) The business model along the involved circular value chain will be defined and the Value Propositions of our Multicycle innovation will be formulated. The logistics and investments for the industrial implementation of the recycling process will be studied. The project knowledge and results will be managed and protected. Business plans and exploitation paths will be elaborated for all Key Exploitable Results (KERs) to map the post-project phase. Business and marketing strategies will be established. Performance Indicators: By M36, final plans will be in place for the transition to the post-project phase and reaching the market, as well as the plan to successfully integrate MultiCycle in future waste management practices and circular value chains. 2 patent applications will have been filed to support partners’ optimal competitive commercial position.
1.2 Relation to the work programme  
The relation of the project to the specific challenges and scope addressed by the topic CE-SPIRE-10-2018 Efficient recycling processes for plastic containing materials (IA), as part of ‘Leadership in enabling and industrial technologies – Nanotechnologies, Advanced Materials, Biotechnology and Advanced Manufacturing and Processing' work programme are outlined in table 1 below: 
Table 1: How MultiCycle relates to the scope and challenges of the CE-SPIRE-10-2018 topic 
Scope/challenges  How MultiCycle addresses them 
Raw materials of fossil origin 
(e.g. PE, PP, and PET) […], bio-
based plastics. Sustainable 
raw materials... 
The CreaSolv® Process used as a basis for our project is extremely versatile while adjusting the selected 
solvents’ solubility parameters towards the target polymer fraction. This will therefore allow us to recycle a 
number of raw materials of fossil origins, such as PE, PP, PA, and PET, as well as bio-based: bio-PA, bio-
PE, bio-PET (which can be used in mixed batches with their fossil counterparts) & PLA (at lab scale for the 
later). We focus on polymers with major market volumes as well as on emerging ones. 
Wide range of applications […] 
composites and packaging. 
In addition to engineering, our project is relevant to several SPIRE sectors derived from chemicals and 
plastics which are characterised by a high dependence on fossil raw materials and energy in their production 
and processing technologies and are therefore under greater pressure to shift to circular models. We will in 
particular cover composites and packaging, which on the one hand side represent a large share of plastic 
consumption (40% of plastics are used for short term packaging applications while automotive consumes 
10% of the plastics produced in EU5), and on the other side were selected among their mutli-materials 
subtypes that are currently not recyclable/recycled.  
Reusing plastic waste, 
delaying final energy recovery 
at the end of life material, and 
avoiding landfill. Cover 
processes for the production 
of recyclable materials 
MultiCycle is based on CreaSolv®, a process that can be adjusted to different plastic and composite 
materials and is extremely suited for heterogeneous ones as the different fractions can be separated. Quite 
uniquely vs. more standard mechanical recycling approaches, the SRM recovered from it do not suffer a 
downgrading in plastic properties (without degradation or molecular weight reduction), nor in terms of 
fibre length. These are critical performance benefits that enable (after suitable reprocessing) the production 
of recycled materials that are in turn virtually recyclable an infinite number of times in multiple subsequent

containing plastics...

to utilise waste heterogeneous 
plastic materials, including 
plastic composites, as input to 
allow the recycling and the re-
processing of this widely 
available resource into added 
value products (excluding 
fuels). 
cycles using MultiCycle process. Only a minor fraction of the complex material may have to be valorised for 
energy (if its recovery is not economically viable). This is a dramatic improvement vs. the current situation 
whereby most of the target packaging and composites are either landfilled or used for energy recovery of 
the organic part. In addition to current drying after the CreaSolv® Process, the later use of supercritical 
carbon dioxide (sc-CO2) assisted extrusion will allow the cleaning step to be improved in a process 
intensification approach, as it will be done as part of the normal compounding (encompassing removal of 
VOCs, solid contaminants and required readditivation). This process was evaluated successfully in the 
EXTRUCLEAN LIFE+ project where large plastic containers for transporting toxic and dangerous products 
(paints, lubricants, solvents, etc..) were recycled for their reuse in the same application. 
Develop process technologies 
and process streams, utilising 
plastic waste as starting 
material for the production of 
added value products 
The project tackles the different steps of the recycling process and of the reprocessing of the polymers and 
the fibres recovered from composites into added value materials such as packaging, textiles, composites, 
often suitable for similar uses as in their primary life cycle. Furthermore, as CreaSolv® allows the separation 
of the pure fractions included in mixed input wastes, SRMs can be reformulated freely (so we will not be 
bound to reprocess a bumper into a new bumper, as is often the limiting approach in current recycling 
approaches). This will be an enabler for opening more cross-sectors links and for broadening SRM’s 
potential to substitute virgin materials. As such, we will complement the technical aspects by the study of 
the supply and value chains both in terms of compatibility and enhancements needed for current waste 
collection and management systems. 
Integration with the relevant 
value chains, ensuring the 
secure supply of the raw 
material streams. A clear 
strategy to involve the relevant 
actors along the value chain is 
expected; 
The project consortium includes representatives that cover the basis of the value chains for packaging and 
automotive application, where related companies act as suppliers of wastes and users of the recycled 
materials (further justified in section 2.1.3). WP9 will dedicate one task on defining the circular business 
models. Supported by the DST delivered in WP7, we will assess the optimal paths for the definition of the 
value chains. The DST will recommend optimal network connections and will reveal value supporting 
creative solutions within a sustainable development context. That will also facilitate new cross value chains 
links (eg. polymers recovered from packaging being used as technical textiles for cars). 
Key issues related to the 
quality of the raw (including 
secondary) material streams 
should be covered, and in 
particular the heterogeneity of 
the waste plastic material, as 
well as the wide variety of 
substances contained in 
plastic materials (e.g. 
plasticisers, anti-oxidants, 
etc.). The valorisation of fillers 
or fibres from composites 
should also be covered;  
Quality/specifications of the 
yielded streams ensuring their 
usability by downstream 
industries; 
The post consumer (pc) wastes fed in our recycling process will be obtained from waste management 
companies through ISWA and from GBP so they are fully representative of the complex nature of such 
currently non recyclable mixtures. They will be complemented by post industrial (pi) wastes from the material 
converters in the consortium. Their composition will be analysed in the lab and through specifically designed 
monitoring systems tailored to the needs of each sector (where respective collection systems are totally 
different) with a target to assessing and optimising the performances of the CreaSolv® Process in different 
scenarios (WP4). Beyond the main polymers, all the substances (including additives and non polymer 
materials, see fig. 12 and 13) will be considered and recovered in keeping with verified economic viability 
(FRAUNHOFER already demonstrated the technical feasibility of CreaSolv® to recover or remove specific 
additives like halogenated fire retardants, fibres, plasticizers or colour pigments6,7,8). The process itself does 
not reduce diameter or length of recovered fibres, so that size of recycled fibres depends on 
cutting/shredding of feed material only. The conservation of the fibre sizing agent is also possible in certain 
cases (as proven with diamonds powder and silanes) but it has to be studied for each combination of 
polymer, filler/fibre, sizing agent to ascertain eventual further formulation tuning needed. 
In addition to taking into account the broad range of specifications for the materials to be recovered, we will 
dedicate particular attention to the requirements for the SRM to be useful. As such, from WP1, we will define 
the properties that the recycled materials should fulfil to set the roadmap for reprocessing development in 
WP3. In WP4, the MultiCycle pilot process will be equipped with advanced PAT systems that will allow inline 
quality control of the recovered polymer streams. WP5-6 will demonstrate that the appropriate properties 
are obtained (meeting initial specs) for using the material according to industrial requirements in the 
packaging and automotive fields respectively. In-depth characterisations will be made eg. in terms of 
molecular weight, thermal and processability behaviour, needed for each application. The results, compared 
to virgin polymers, will feature in technical data sheets (TDS). 
Non-technological hurdles, 
such as regulations and 
standards, to enable the 
prompt deployment in industry 
of the developed concepts and 
economic indicators (e.g. 
CAPEX and OPEX). 
The initial CAPEX and OPEX values are already calculated in Section 2.2.2.E based on results existing at 
high TRL on different materials. WP7 includes economic and environmental sustainability studies 
complemented by a techno-economical analysis (TEA) that will lead to refined CAPEX and OPEX when 
projecting the recycling process to a competitive scale. In terms of standards, aspect overseen in WP7, they 
will also be covered in several tasks along the development of the pilot plant in WP4 and regarding the 
reusability of the recycled plastics in packaging in WP5. The effects of existing legislations will be studied: 
e.g. waste legislation leading to reduced land filling of packaging or end of life vehicles (ELV) directives 
requiring OEMs to use recyclable materials are expected to positively push the market uptake of our process 
and materials. We will also embed policy makers recommendations in WP8 for example in terms of 
incentivizing the actual recycling of multi-materials, as well as to reconsider the use of recycled materials in 
food contact since we will show that the material recovered with CreaSolv® is as pure as virgin ones at 
molecular level, so the current regulatory barrier should be limited to standard mechanical recycling. 
Demonstration activities, 
prototypes and pilot 
The MultiCycle pilot process chain will be set up initially in non industrial environment (WP2-3) and will then 
be demonstrated in a real industrial environment (WP4-6). The large MultiCycle pilot plant (TRL7) will be run

The project includes a number of actions in WP8 to generate a broad collaborationframework  with relevant 
initiatives, such as other projects funded in the same call topic, the NMBP26 call and other calls identified in 
the new EU plastic strategy, as well as with on going projects where partners participate, CEFLEX among 
others. MultiCycle will also engage with relevant activities through ISWA recycling working groups, as well 
as other associations linked to the Advisory Board (AB). V2020 will establish a Circular Plastics Helix that 
will form the basis of our engagement with other stakeholders and initiatives, and will give very high visibility 
to MultiCycle. End users are involved within the consortium, and will be also reached via the AB, the Circular 
Plastics Helix, the Capacity Building programme as well as through a DST that will service towards the 
support of economic feasibility and environmental sustainability of selected circular recycling paths and the 
reuse of materials recycled from MultiCycle.  
In terms of generated data, the project will subscribe to the Open Data Programme and deliver a Data 
Management Plan early in the project (by M6) that will allow partners to have an outmost curation of the data 
(many of the process data will be directly fed from a process analytical platform and used for process 
optimisation) and internal use (eg. as a source for the model-based DST), as well as to provide non 
patentable data to external parties with the aim of extending the deployment of MultiCycle so that an 
increasing amount of multi-materials becomes recycled in the post project phase. The open access database 
(under a license) will support the replicability of the project results.

The fit to the Focus area: Connecting economic and environmental gains - the Circular Economy (CE) and more especially to the Plastic strategy in a circular economy9 package released on 16/01/2018 is further justified in Section 2.
1.3  Concept and methodology 
(a) Overall concept underpinning the Project The European plastics industry plays a vital role in the EU economy, with 1.45 million employees and a turnover of 350 billion (including converters and machine building producers). The current plastic recycling business is sizeable with 1000 companies, 30.000 employees, a capacity of 3 million tonnes recycled per year and a €2 Bn turnover10. Nevertheless, if we consider that each year in Europe around 25.8 Mio tonnes of post-consumer plastic wastes are generated, with less than 30% being used for material recycling, 31% ending up in landfill and 40% being used for energy recovery, the opportunity to further unlock high value applications from this waste is clear. Established processes present numerous deficiencies in the recycling of our target multi-materials and recycling is currently typically limited to post-industrial waste reprocessing or energy valorisation, therefore highlighting the need for significant improvements throughout the waste management chain.  In terms of packaging material recycling, monomaterial rigid bottles, cups and containers, usually made with petrochemical polymers, are currently the most separately collected and recycled packaging, with chains and final products established on the market. In a context where the plastics from post-consumer waste are sorted by separating the PET bottles and the PP/PE rigid container fraction11, high barrier packages with a multilayer structure represent an issue. The residual part, recovered in the bottom of the plant, consists of mixed plastics that can represent as much as 40% of the total pc plastic. Usually multilayer packages, but also monolayer films, are present in this fraction going to incineration.  In terms of automotive, End-of-Life Vehicles generate an estimated ca. 8 M tons of waste annually in the EU12. After decontamination of vehicles, the remaining structure and indoor elements are shredded and metal is recovered. Remaining materials like ELVs’ plastics, composites, foams and textiles, glass, wood, wires, little metal parts, little electronics and rubber, known as ‘light leftovers’ (<200 kg/m3 apparent density), are also indiscriminately and independently of their values incinerated or even landfilled if PVC is included13. Such practices do not help meeting ELV directive targets in terms of materials recycling >85-95% of materials. Among ELV materials entering the dismantling processes, mostly metals are recovered and directed for either, reuse, remanufacturing or recycling, so improvements are clearly needed for plastics & composites14,15.  Separation and valorisation of polymer based multi-materials is challenging. It has been addressed by a specific solvent-based process (Figure 2) that has been developed and patented by the Fraunhofer Institute for Process Engineering 
 
9 https://ec.europa.eu/commission/publications/documents-strategy-plastics-circular-economy_en  10 http://www.plasticsrecyclers.eu/facts-figures  11 Coltelli M.B., Aglietto M., Riutilizzo dei materiali polimeri, Edizioni Nuova Cultura, 2015 12 J. Heiskanen et al. (2013). “A look at the European Union’s End-of-Life Vehicle Directive - Challenges of treatment and disposal in Finland”. 13 GHK. (2006).                          CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 15 of 117 
and Packaging IVV, Germany. The proprietary CreaSolv® Process uses proprietary trade secrets solvent formulations with a low risk for both users and the environment, and ideally not to be classified according to Globally Harmonised System (GHS) criteria. Solvents used consist of a range of adequately selected (polymer specific) polar/apolar and protic/aprotic solvents. These selective solvents-mixtures extract the target pure polymers from heterogeneous waste input streams at subcritical conditions, allowing the polymer solution to be selectively separated from undissolved components or harmful and/or prohibited pollutants, such as plasticizers or halogenic flame retardants.16,17 At the end of the process, the solvent is recovered by drying in order to ensure closed-loop circulation and to keep the process economically viable. The recycled polymer is solvent free, meets the properties of virgin material, and is a suitable substitute material in production processes. The conservation of additives/fillers in the polymer can also be designed on a case by case basis. In general, the CreaSolv® Process comprises the following unit operations:  1. Dissolution of a target polymer from a plastic rich input fraction, e.g. composite  2. Purification of the dissolved polymers from dissolved and undissolved matter (non-target polymers, foreign materials…) 3. Precipitation is an optional first mechanical step to separate polymer from solvents 4. Drying of the polymer by separation and re-circulation of the applied solvent formulation 5. Treatment of the solvents collected in purification, precipitation and drying steps and recovery for complete re-use.  6. Granulation We refer to the MultiCycle process chain as the overall steps for handling the multi-materials to be recycled, from the waste collected (which will be monitored before entering the process) until the production of all the new target fractions ready for use, encapsulating the CreaSolv® Process repeated a number of times, which will be upscaled and improved, and optimised reprocessing steps that will also be adjusted to the polymer and fibres thereof. It will be applied on fractions with complex compositions that are currently not recycled by existing recycling plants. 
 Fig. 2: General CreaSolv® and MultiCycle process scheme (subsequent steps can also allow the separation of further fillers contained in 
each recovered polymer, like for organic additives dissolved with the polymer containing them, which can be removed or not, as preferred) 
Purification is performed by mechanical and extractive means and removes insoluble matter like non-target plastics, dust, metals, alumina, printing inks, etc. Compared to other recycling processes, the CreaSolv® Process has already shown to achieve higher purity and mechanical properties of recovered single polymer fractions. It can source polymers from waste fractions that are not suitable for state-of-the-art recycling processes.18  Further reprocessing steps also bear a fundamental role in warrantying the properties of the recovered polymers are suitable for the application (in standard recycling processes, the amount of recycled materials is very limited). Using enhancement processes, and possibly compensating any degradation occurred during first life cycle, such as sc-CO2 injection during compounding as well as the addition of selected additives, our target is to reach a content of recycled polymer as high as possible (target>80%) in the products obtained from recycled materials, to avoid the need for blending SRMs with virgin polymer (only minor additives will not be from recycled origin). In terms of recycling demonstration on packaging waste, as seen, we will focus on currently non recycled materials that include flexible mono and multilayer packaging, as well as rigid ones that are multilayer or use a low volume material that does not make a separate recycling line economically feasible. Statistics provided by ISWA will bring a clear picture of available non-recycled fraction depending on the country so the used waste compositions are representative of different cases existing around EU (due to variable collecting and recycling systems). Based on AMCOR (world leader in packaging)’s list of 10 highest volumes materials that would fall under this “difficult to impossible” to recycle category in the current situation, we shortlisted a number of representative complex materials including different combinations of laminated or coextruded polymers layers like PET, PE, PP, but also barrier made of Aluminum foil, oriented PA (OPA), metalised PET, physical vapour deposited (PVD) Silicon oxide SiOx or Aluminum Oxide AlOx on PET, EVOH (ethylene vinyl alcohol), as well as in cases paper layers, and of course inks, tie layers and adhesives as needed respectively for printing, co-extrusion and lamination. We will recycle all major polymers found in this mixture: PA, PE, PET, PP but also PLA as a case of emerging material (further details section 2.1.1, fig. 12).  Packaging demonstrators from SRM are planned to be produced with at least 70% of recycled content (using virgin materials only to replace the materials that were not viable to recover, and allowing a bit of flexibility of design) in small prototype series in WP5 by AMCOR by means of extrusion, vapour deposition and lamination processes, which are commonly used processes by the converters in the packaging industry. The evaluation of the processability of the SRMs will be performed by the production of mono- and multi-layered structures for food, and non-food packaging applications including personal and home care products (18 monolayer films, 6 coextruded films, 8 multi-layer laminates and end packaging format). The packaging demonstrators for food will not have the processed SRM (except if from pi waste) directly contacting the food due to current legislative barriers, although their suitability for such purpose will also be tested.  In terms of recycling demonstration on composites waste, we will mainly focus on FRP materials of relevance in the automotive sector, including materials compatible with very different types of processes such as thermoforming/compression moulding of non-woven of long fibres, continuous fibres composite sheets, over-moulding with short fibre reinforced compounds, etc. The target will be to recover the polymers, e.g. PP or PA of different types, as the main thermoplastic matrix used in the sector but also GF as well as CF from emerging composites used for now predominantly in sport & electrical cars or other sectors. The availability of cheaper and sufficiently long r-CF will enable new opportunities for CF-based composites (CFC) to be opened up, especially when combined with recycled polymer matrices. Since MultiCycle will recover pure fractions, the SRMs can be recombined in new structures in a flexible fashion as desired by the converters. Composites demonstrators with at least 70% of recycled content are planned to be produced from the SRM (coming mainly from recycled composites but also from recycled packaging-derived textiles) in small prototype series in WP6. SILON will produce textiles based on pure r-PET and r-PA. Non-wovens will be produced by ELG using r-CF (up to 100 mm), optionally with a variable amount of PA fibres (20-80% ratio vs. CF). Continuous fibre-reinforced thermoplastic sheets will be produced by Bond-Laminates (BONDL). Standard reinforcements (plain or twill weaves) will be impregnated with the recycled polymer to obtain GF+r-PP, GF+r-PA and CF+r-PA sheets' (with a fibre volume content of ca. 45%). The different semi-finished products will be used in semi-structural and structural automotive applications and will be processed via SCM, insert or hybrid moulding at FARPLAS and TOFAS. In terms of inter-disciplinary considerations and use of stakeholder knowledge, our trans-disciplinary team combines all the relevant skills and organisations needed to address the full extent of the challenge of demonstrating innovative

The latter cover the required supply and value chain: plastic/ packaging/ composites suppliers/ converters/ recyclers (ARKEMA, SILON, AMCOR, BONDL, ELG, GBP, FARPLAS, TOFAS), technology providers of recycling equipment and process control and monitoring systems (LOEMI, IRIS respectively), LCA, LCC and TEA techno-economic assessment (VERTECH). Through ISWA, MultiCycle has a significant policy outreach capacity to raise environmental political awareness, which, in addition to the ground-breaking performance and competitive advantages, could further encourage the adoption of our new solutions. End users actually have a key role in supplying post-industrial and representative post-consumer wastes (complemented by pc waste streams from ISWA, GPB among others) and using recycled materials. Furthermore, additional stakeholders will be involved in an Advisory Board (AB) consisting of well selected members, each with a specific role (summarised before their support letters, annexed to the bid after Section 5) including 8 European & national associations and 3 SMEs in complementary sectors to the consortium: FPE, CEFLEX, EuCIA, EUBP, PACKCLUSTER, IVLV, ZLV, ANARPLA, ARCTIC, DIAD, AEROCIRCULAR. They will be consulted as necessary for both technical (possibly providing samples, assessing the relevant of the results for their case), policy and business-related insights and further AB members may also be appointed if needed. Positioning of the project In terms of TRL, the main activities performed in MultiCycle span from validating the technology in an industrially relevant environment (TRL5) to demonstration in operational (TRL6) and relevant (TRL7) environments. The majority of the technologies involved in the project have already been validated thoroughly at pilot scale in RTD environments based on prior research from involved partners, and in some cases on other relatively similar materials. This will help to focus the efforts where required. It is deemed realistic to state that all the main processes and the resulting secondary raw materials reuse will be demonstrated at TRL7 to reach the ambitious project goal. When considering the whole value chain underpinning the MultiCycle project, it is clear that we will start from relatively mature processing technologies and combine them with breakthrough process innovations. Therefore, main TRL advancements are focusing on the recycling routes, while the re-processing techniques, generally already standardly used within the involved industrial sectors for virgin materials, as well as the further conversion processes, will need very little adaptation to process our SRMs. The absence of commercially available multilayer packaging or composites from close to 100% SRM is due to the insufficient properties/ high cost of currently available recycled materials, not to a technology gap. (b) Methodology Our approach and methodology involve two main components: 1) Technical piloting & Demonstration: After upscaling the process, we will extensively demonstrate the feasibility of the recycling of selected packaging and composite case studies (summarised in fig. 12 and 13, section 2 and WP5-6). In addition, the demonstration work will focus on reprocessing tailored formulations and production of new packaging and automotive parts. The performance of the demonstrators and process to obtain them will be evaluated by environmental and TEA using LCA & LCC methodologies among others, since it is essential to use precise analysis of the impacts to back up the claim of improved environmental impact.  2) Innovation: This work will accompany and cross-cut the innovation action with the aim of preparing for post-project market replication of the project results. It consists of the following activities: a) analysis of the market, regulatory, social & safety aspects that could impact the future commercialisation of the proposed technologies leading to the drawing of guidelines and recommendations used in lobbying with retailers and policy makers in relation with new potential to improve resource and waste management. products & processes will be studied according to applicable legislations throughout the action. How sex and/or gender analysis is taken into account in the project’s content Innovation will consider the gender dimension and cater for the needs, motivations and differences of females where applicable, inspired by how taking gender into account has yielded new research results and sparked creativity, opening new avenues for future research for those who give it serious attention19. It will be relevant in the consumer-specific communication activities regarding the MultiCycle resulting products: the consideration of environmental factors on packaging selection is likely to vary depending on the gender. In addition, although the plastic and recycling industries consist of a majority of males, it is clear that women are predominantly involved both in the purchase and waste sorting at household level. As such, our project will consider the relevance of the gender dimension in communication & training, to ensure this is picked up in the right message as appropriate to reach both males and females. This is important in ensuring the education of the current professional and next generation of scientists, technologist and others needed in the EU to foster the future of the CE and maximise its creativity and innovation potential. The gender dimension of our consortium and our measures to align with the article 16 of the H2020 programme are presented at the end of section 3.3.
1.4 Ambition State of the art and progresses in the field of Plastic-based packaging recycling There are few positive examples of recycling plastics from packaging applications: 59.8% of the petrochemical based PET going to bottles or containers were recycled in 201620, and mono-material packaging made of polyolefins as well as some grades of mixed plastics can be used for downgraded applications at lower cost than the virgin grades they compete with. When it comes to more speciality materials, however, such as the plastic-based multilayer structures used in packaging, there is generally no current industrial solution for their recycling. One major advantage of the multilayer packaging is the possibility of reduction in costs by combining different types of layers having different functionalities (e.g. oxygen/moisture barrier, UV-protection, printability, mechanical protection, or sealability) at reduced layer thicknesses in comparison to the layer thicknesses needed from a single layer to perform the same function. They are environmentally advantageous due to the low use of materials and energy, low carbon footprint, and reduction in food waste. However, because of their poor recyclability, today most multilayers typically are incinerated or end up in landfill.  Low-, high-density polyethylene (LDPE, HDPE), PP, and PET make up the largest share of plastics used in packaging, followed by polystyrene (PS) and other plastics such as PLA21, 22. Polyolefins are used for sealability and moisture barrier. PA is used for mechanical stability, and oriented PET films provide an excellent surface for printing. A two-layer packaging consists of a sealing layer facing the product (e.g. LDPE), and an outer layer (e.g. PET) for mechanical resistance, printability or barrier. A three-layer structure includes an additional barrier layer such as an inorganic layer, e.g. metallised Al, or SiOx coated on the PET or PP substrate used outside. An adhesive layer is also needed for lamination of these coated layers on sealing layer. In some cases, barrier layers such as EVOH, or PA6 are used in the centre of multi-layered structure. They need to be adhered to the sealing layer and to the outer layer by means of adhesives.23, 24, 25  The EoL treatment of pc-plastic packaging waste includes collection, sorting and reprocessing steps. After sorting, they are reprocessed in 3 ways: (i) Incineration; recovery of electrical or thermal energy from polymers, no material delivery and production of toxic gases, and residue is another big disadvantage 26, (ii) Mechanical recycling; processing the plastic waste with shredding, dissolving or melting combined with subsequent purification techniques26, 27, . The disadvantage is the deterioration of properties in each cycle due to degradation or insufficient cleaning of the recycled polymer, (iii) Chemical

                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 20 of 117 
recycling26, where plastic waste is turned back into its oil/hydrocarbon components, is not the preferable circular approach. For multilayer packaging composites, especially flexible ones, there is no high-quality recycling established currently in EU. Even recycling of flexible monolayer packaging is challenging because it is difficult to sort, convey and process by recyclers, who are more prepared for rigid packaging. As a consequence, most material is used for energy recovery only, which is achieved by incineration plants with an average efficiency of 35%, or land filled depending on the country. Currently, multilayers are usually an impurity and have to be separated from the presorted input in an additional separation step, thereby it is crucial to deliver a solution for their recycling. Today, there are 2 ways to recycle multi-layered packaging structures28; (1) to process the components together in one compatibilization step, (2) to separate the different multilayer components and make them available for recycling in separated recycling streams. The first option, compatibilization, can be used, however, only for recycling of pi-plastic waste, because the composition of the waste input needs to be known, and kept constant, for the selection of the optimum type and amount of the compatibiliser. One example is the recycling case of PET/PE bilayer, widely used for packing fresh foods. It can only be recycled by grinding and then extrusion. However, PET and PE are immiscible and their compatibility must be improved for high-value applications by using proper compatibilisers29,30,31 but the resulting cost is not viable. Furthermore, it was observed that the presence of the adhesive, often poly(ethylene-co-vinyl acetate) (EVA), between the two layers can result in yellowing of PET/PE blended granules32 due to EVA degradation. Another example is multilayers containing polyolefins and a barrier layer of EVOH that cannot be separated and is incompatible with PE and PP33 during recycling. Therefore, only recycled products with low mechanical properties are obtained. Additionally, the only possible EoL treatment of these recycled polymers is incineration, leading again to a limited scope of its application for multilayers.  The second option, separation of different components, can be performed by 1) delamination, or by 2) recycling of polymers by dissolution methods: The delamination can be performed physically by the dissolution of macromolecules, and mechanically or chemically by the decomposition of an interlayer. For the recycling of multilayer pi-waste34,35 a delamination process has been proposed. This approach addresses the removal/dissolution of the adhesive layers of multilayer packaging. It results in separation of the components of a multilayered structure, but requires a subsequent separation of the segregated films by density, NIR or electrostatic. The technology is provided in technical scale by the German company Saperatec GmbH35 and uses a micro emulsion to separate composite films. However, the application of this concept is not yet universal, as many different adhesives are used in the multilayer market, making it not possible to apply for recycling of pc-plastic waste. One of the most promising and advanced approaches is the recycling of polymers by dissolution method. The solvent based CreaSolv® Technology, developed by FRAUNHOFER IVV36,37 uses specific solvents to dissolve one polymer type out of a waste stream, including mixed polymers and polymer-based composites. Multi-step procedures allow step-by-step dissolution of different components of a multilayer and recover the polymers in high purity by removal of impurities and recirculation of the solvent. Applied to packaging waste, the CreaSolv® Technology so far targeted PE and PP mainly, resulting in a quality close to that of the virgin material. CreaSolv® does not require extensively sorted infeed materials. The waste can be a mixture of different multilayer and non-multilayer packaging systems, making the technology appropriate for the recycling of real pc-packaging waste including high shares of multilayer packaging items, thereby reducing waste and increasing overall valorisation of multilayer films. One major drawback of this technology is that the technology is not yet available at industrial scale and positive business cases have not been stated in operational practise, drawbacks which will be overcome thanks to this MultiCycle project.  The increased use of bio-based plastics having by far the highest growth rate among all packaging materials (in comparison to the overall, the market volume is still quite low; forecasted market share is 1.7 % in 2021, a niche product) will result in new types of materials entering into the established ways of plastic waste management. Legislation in some EU countries (Belgium, Netherlands, Luxembourg, Italy) actively support biopolymers. The EU Directive regarding the reduction of the consumption of lightweight plastic carrier bags is another law that boosts the use of biodegradable films in some countries. These regulations increase the opportunities for new packages designed for recycling, being made of recycled raw materials as well as from renewable resources. Bio-based plastics with identical chemical structures, e.g. the partially bio-based PET (Bio-PET) or the near 100% bio-based polyethylene (Bio-PE), undergo the same recycling processes as their fossil-based equivalents. PLA, which is one of the mostly used biopolymers in packaging (100% bio-based, and very versatile), was the

focus of a study (SUSPLA) initiated by the German Ministry of Food and Agriculture (BMEL). The research alliance produced pc- as well as pi-waste containing PLA and tested the feasibility of the material and feedstock recycling processes accompanied by LCAs. The CreaSolv® Process was implemented to the heterogeneous waste mixture of pc-PLA waste with about 30% impurities. The recyclates were processed by means of injection moulding tests and no differences were observed in comparison to the results obtained by using the virgin PLA material. It has been demonstrated that with an approximately 50% higher yield, the CreaSolv® Process offers substantial economic advantages compared to conventional material recycling. This is due to the process not requiring prior pre-treatment steps of density separation and air separation. Although NIR can sort PLA from PET, the presence of minor amounts of PLA in PET waste can lead to degradation and PET recyclers’ reluctance, which has hindered the market entry of PLA. CreaSolv® can separate small fractions of mixed polyesters, thereby resolving such issue.  Beyond the state of the art, in MultiCycle the most commonly used polymers in multi-layered packaging -PE, PP, PET and PA- will be recovered by means of the CreaSolv® Process in MultiCycle upscaled plant (PLA will be recovered at lab scale only due to insufficient amount in pi/pc waste). These target polymers will be selectively dissolved from the plastic waste and contaminants will be removed from the solution using special purification methods. Customized solvent formulations will be applied in combination with the novel designed CreaSolv® plant to recover PA and PET with properties similar to the virgin polymers, not recovered so far from pc-waste. Single-, and multi-layer packaging films and laminates used in new demonstrator packages designed with high recycling potential, will be produced from the recycled polymers using industrial converting lines; such as film extrusion, physical vapor deposition, coating and adhesive lamination processes. The usability of the plastics recovered by the MultiCycle processes at content > 70% and their ability to meet applicable legislations and industrial requirements for packaging will be confirmed. Safety compliance will be ensured for the recyclates obtained from pc-waste and pi-waste for food and non-food applications.  State of the art and progresses in the field of recycling processes for Fibre Reinforced FRP The recycling of carbon fibre thermoset composites is economically viable due to the high cost of virgin carbon fibres and the good quality of the recycled carbon fibres. The lower cost of recycled carbon fibres allows industries, such as the automotive industry- who previously, due to financial limitations, could not use virgin carbon fibres- to consider recycled carbon fibres as a lightweight solution. Two typical processes for valorising carbon fibre composites are pyrolysis and solvolysis38, of which the most advanced and most commercially used is the pyrolysis process, with or without oxygen. Waste in the form of prepregs, dry fibres or hardened laminates are processed in temperatures between 450 and 700 °C. The waste is converted into fibres, oils and gases. The oil contains monomers that in principle can be reused in new resin. However, due to economic constraints, extraction of monomers is not carried out in practice. After shredding the recyclate, it typically consists of fibres (up to 150 mm) that can be used in the compounding industry as chopped fibres, milled powder or as nonwovens. The process is currently at an optimisation phase in which the gas treatment systems are gradually being altered and fine-tuned. For example, project partner ELG, managed to lower processing energy consumption in 2015 by 35% by improving their gas treatment process and is planning on establishing a new abatement system (2018) to increase throughput while limiting the gas released in the atmosphere. Solvolysis is a recycling technology in which a solvent dissolves the composite matrix at lower temperatures than pyrolysis (but still up to 300ºC possibly also generating some CF degradation) and was mainly developed for thermosetting matrices. Commercial applications currently remain limited, even though it appears to be a very promising route making it possible to retain the fibre structure, as well as the plastic monomers. Unlike the pyrolysis process, this process also avoids the formation of carbonized materials, which contaminates the fibre surface and as such prevents good fibre matrix adhesion. Besides pyrolysis and solvolysis, other techniques are being studied such as electrodynamic fragmentation, grinding/pelletising of the composite waste to be used in injection moulding and direct re-forming, but none of these reached so far maturity due to economic and/or processing reasons (e.g. harmful carbon dust production during grinding).39 Compared to standard solvolysis processes, as dissolution temperatures in CreaSolv® are typically below solvolysis temperatures, effects on fibre diameters are lower40. For pyrolysis, a reduction of fibre diameters has been reported.  In contrast to CF, it is more difficult to apply pyrolysis to glass fibres. At a pyrolysis temperature of 500 °C, the residual strength of the glass fibres is already decreased to less than 50%. The high temperatures degrade the sizing of the glass fibre and the glass fibre structure.41 Studies show that the current most efficient way of valorising glass fibre composites is by processing them in cement ovens. Milled material is mixed with other raw materials in which 30% of the composite is used as the source of energy and 70 % as the raw material. This process is however not economically attractive. Also mechanical milling technologies are commercially available, but only on a small scale and the GF abrasion leads to high intensive energy & resources consumption.

Beyond the state of the art, MultiCycle will: produce recycled carbon fibres with superior lengths, with properties similar to those from the primary fibres, and which will be structured into nonwovens. Also recycled glass fibres with high quality will be recovered. Contrary to the current state-of-the-art, no downcycling to less demanding applications (e.g. plastic filler) is anticipated. There is also a good tolerance for the presence of other materials on the composite part like paints, adhesives... Moreover, contrary to the standard composites recycling processes, the thermoplastic matrix will be recovered as well and reprocessed to textile fibres that will be reused as matrix in composites or applied in the textile industry.  State of the art and progresses in the field of waste plastic identification/ monitoring Packaging material recycling from mixed waste plastics is challenging and based on sophisticated process chains combining different sorting steps like screening, wind sifting, NIR sorting, removal of metals and others. The main purpose is to separate the recyclable mono-materials like PE, PP, PET for further mechanical recycling, but mainly in their rigid form since flexible counterparts, even monolayer films, are difficult to handle by recyclers. Equipment to automatically identify plastics, are already widely commercially available e.g. via spectroscopic identification systems, including near-infrared, X-ray, colour, or laser, and combinations thereof42, but currently many recyclers are still sorting plastics by hand which is unreliable, expensive, and not suitable for transparent flexible films. Due to the need to sort plastics by colour for mechanical reprocessing, camera or visible spectroscopy are often used as a first sorting step. Therefore most state of the art devices are used to identify the “wanted” rigid fraction and the rest including multilayer/films, aimed at incineration, are generally not the purpose of the sorting systems. IRIS has experience in developing customised fast online VIS/NIR spectroscopic tools for plastic processing monitoring to precisely identify their composition and traces of unwanted substances43,44. Results from IRIS show hyperspectral imaging (HSI)’s capacity to distinguish coloured from natural HDPE and from LDPE from multilayer films including PE among its layers using chemometrics analysis selecting different principal components (fig. 5)44.  In terms of composites from ELVs, since light fractions are normally handled together, the most common identification technique in place during the car dismantling process, based on induction sensors, is for detecting metals. An added difficulty for automatic optical sorting of automotive parts is that the majority are black/ use carbon black preventing the penetration of NIR. Different emerging techniques such as TeraHertz45 and Laser Induced breakdown sprectroscopy (LIBs)46,47 have though been reported to allow determining the composition of different plastics even in black colour. In contrast to general recycling processes, the perfect pre-sorting of materials is not needed in the case of MultiCycle process that is tolerant to mixtures, nevertheless, in view of the variable compositions in the packaging fraction nowadays rejected by the recyclers, having a composition monitoring system will be a fundamental facilitator for the optimal set up of the process (e.g. predicting automatically if all dissolution steps implemented in the recycling plant should be activated or not in cases whereby in the batch in question, the content of e.g. PA is below a threshold that does not warrant the viability of its recovery). As such, we will optimise the hardware and software for the waste monitoring system thanks to advanced chemometrics analysis and combining several spectroscopic technologies (possibly NIR and visible hyperspectral imaging) to go beyond the conventional NIR results in order to provide accurate reliable compositional information even of monolayer and flexible films via data fusion. For composites, the project will deliver an innovative handheld monitoring system (based on TeraHertz or LIBs and possibly coupling UV-VIS) also using optimised chemiometrics approach, to monitor the type of plastic used in different composite parts and identify those worth recycling separately through MultiCycle process.

State of the art and progresses in the status of implementation of the CreaSolv® Process The basics of the patented CreaSolv® Process concept were already described in Section 1.3. Here we will cover its main applications and its technical readiness for the project end uses. The process has been applied to ABS, PS and PC/ABS from WEEE, PP from ELV, PA from fibre composites and other waste streams. In studies with WEEE plastics, CreaSolv® was able to remove brominated flame retardants dioxins and furans below levels of concern48,49. It was reported that CreaSolv® is a substantially better environmental option than landfill and also a better option than incineration with energy recovery as it is a low energy consumption process and requires rather simple process equipment. In the PolyREssource project, the CreaSolv® Process was further optimized in terms of productivity and selectivity (e.g. dissolving ABS and PS consecutively) and it provided product qualities well acceptable for downstream polymer processing. PA has been subjected to CreaSolv® Processes in a series of public projects and small scale feasibility studies, amongst others aiming at PA 6.12, PA 6 or PA 6.6.50 Obtained recycled PA types were evaluated positively and considered to replace virgin PA in colour-insensitive applications. A recent evaluation of the economy of the CreaSolv® for styrenic polymers revealed comparably low process cost (70-80% of the expected revenues from the products when calculating SRM prices with 75% of virgin).  Existing production capacity is available at the small technical line at FRAUNHOFER IVV with a research related capacity of 5-10 kg a day (fig. 6) and some single unit operations can be performed in technical scale aggregates of LOEMI, however, the latter are not combined to a complete line. There is no CreaSolv® Plant at industrial or commercial scale yet. However, in 2018, two processes will be installed at industrial scale, one plant by UNILEVER for post-consumer multilayers packaging originating from South-east Asian landfills. Another plant will be run by the PolystyreneLoop Corporation to recycle PS from waste EPS from Construction and Demotion sources that contains the persistent organic pollutant HBCD, so a different field from those covered in MultiCycle. The multilayer plant in Asia, however, will only extract PE as main fraction from the waste stream. PP, PA, or PET, which are also expected in these waste streams are not intended to be valorised there and require an adapted CreaSolv® Process, which is the scope of MultiCycle. Besides, there is no industrial plant available for the solvent based separation of fibre reinforced plastics, despite the emerging markets for these products and the increasing amounts of post-industrial waste streams.   Beyond the state of the art, MultiCycle will design and install a CreaSolv® Pilot plant which allows flexible demonstration of the innovative process for both target waste streams, recovering main polymers and fibres from multilayers and FRP. The plant concept will reach a capacity of 25 kg /h (so 600kg/ day) vs. the current 5-10 kg /day and will be equipped with process automation technologies, enabling mass and energy balanced trials as well as transparent and optimal process control. To optimise the process and its cost effectiveness, advanced Industry 4.0 digital solutions will support the upscaling of the recycling process. A number of sensors will be installed along the MultiCycle process chain resulting in data that will be gathered in a PAT platform to feed data fusion models allowing relevant process decision making. MEMS viscosity sensors will be used to track the end point of the dissolution of a given fraction, and will enable the cycle time to be optimised, while triggering the next CreaSolv® step automatically. In addition, the melted output polymer will also be monitored by NIR (among others) in terms of absence of unwanted mixed polymer, additives, particles and of VOC content. Predictive models will facilitate the process set up based on the mining of the data from previous recycling trials and the monitored input composition. Depending on the exploitation scope, the predictive models could also allow the initial process set up to be adjusted to maximise given targets of e.g. selective recovery of certain fractions.  State of the art and progresses in the polymer reprocessing. Plastics materials are formulated with complex blends of materials collectively known as additives. These are used to improve among others the impact strength, clarity, chemical and heat resistance, weather resistance and colour retention properties of base polymers. They also aid in the processing of plastics by increasing melt strength, heat stability and lubricity 
 
48 Schlummer et al, Recycling of flame-retarded plastics from waste electric and electronic equipment (WEEE). Waste Manag.                          CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 24 of 117 
to help plastics processors to achieve greater output rates and increased efficiency without loss of quality51.  During its life time or within the recycling, some of the additives could be removed or deactivated (by oxidizing processes) and depending on the secondary material’s application, it may be necessary to add some additional additives. The type and amount of additives to use depends on the expected requirements but at least antioxidants, internal lubricant and heat stability are required since they will have lost properties during the first process. The additives will be added to the recovered polymer during melt processing using a twin-screw extruder or in a single screw extruder using additives as a masterbatch. Although Creasolv® does not lead to further polymer degradation, during the plastic primary use, polymer can be degraded and require regenerating. Regeneration consists in repairing the polymeric backbone by joining the sides of different broken polymer chains and increasing the molecular weight. As a consequence, the mechanical properties, melt strength and viscosity are increased. Amongst others, this backbone reconstruction is based on the use of chain extenders52 as additives53. Although several technologies exist to reclaim degraded resins, use of chain extender is a new simple, efficient, and low-cost alternative54. Chain extenders have direct influence on the distribution of both hard and soft segments in the polymer. The degrees of crystallinity and crosslinking are also affected by the structure and chemical composition of the chain extender. These can be used to rebuild the polymer chains in plastics polymerized via condensation (such as polyesters or polyamides), ultimately restoring the melt strength of the polymer sufficient for stable processing (figure 7), increase its hydrolytic stability and mechanical strength. They could be used if degradation is detected after the CreaSolv® Process due to first life cycle or if the secondary application requires longer chains. Additionally, some studies demonstrated that chain extenders also improve the compatibility of plastic with different melt flow index. Supercritical (sc) fluids provide the ideal conditions for extracting compounds with a high degree of recovery in a short period of time. In general, sc-CO2 technology is much cleaner and safer than conventional processes and preserves the active ingredients as well as the characteristics of the substances. AIMPLAS has a sc-CO2 technology tandem recycling plant (figure 8) to improve the performance of plastics (e.g. removing odour components in natural fibre compounds, cleaning polymers and helping additives dispersion). It has been shown that sc-CO2 compatibilises polymer blends and produces morphological changes in the polymer, improving the specific surface and effectiveness of the coupling agents and other additives. The use of CO2 in a continuous process (e.g. compounding) allows to: i) reduce the viscosity of the plastic blend in order to improve the interaction of immiscible polymers and in consequence, the efficiency of chain extenders, ii) dissolve and remove gases formed during the extrusion steps due to contaminants or polymer additives degradation, iii) reduce the amount of required additives and iv) increase extruder output. CO2 injected to the extruder can be recovered, cleaned and pressurized to be reused in the extrusion process avoiding any CO2 or VOCs emissions. The FP7 CLIPP55 and LIFE EXTRUCLEAN56 projects aimed at recycling package polyolefin materials that contain volatile substances, have demonstrated important advantages of sc-CO2 in comparison with standard extrusion process, not only in terms of final plastic quality, also in energy and cost savings.  Beyond the state of the art, MultiCycle aims to reformulate the recycled materials (PET, PA, PE, PP) coming from the CreaSolv® Process by continuous melt process (compounding) assisted with sc-CO2, to remove any VOC and improve additives dispersion. The recycled polymers will be suitable to be processed by extrusion and injection moulding and fulfil all the requirements of end-users. If it is necessary, polymer regeneration will be achieved by the use of a synergic combination of chain extenders and antioxidants in order to achieve the required melt strength, viscosity and properties to be reused in the food (pc waste-based SRM as sealing layer) or non-food packaging and automotive sectors. 
 
Innovation potential  Addressing our societal and environmental challenges, e.g. the depletion of non-renewable resources and sub-optimal end of life compatibility of current materials with existing waste management scenarios, is recognized to be a major catalyst for
