increasing our innovation capacity, and for the integration of new knowledge generated as a result of research into our practical societal and industrial contexts. The CE also unlocks tremendous potential for the development of innovative new materials % processed. The global paradigm shift from take-make-dispose linear model towards a Circular model represents a unique opportunity for Europe and the European Process Industries to kick-start a world leading competitive CE with significant economic, environmental and societal benefits.  The broad innovation potential from MultiCycle results arises from delivering solutions tapping into currently unmet market needs, as well as facilitating enforcement of existing environmental legislations such as ELV and packaging waste management directives (94/62/CE and 2004/12/CE) in an inclusive EU economy (target <10% landfill in municipal solid wastes by 2030). The expected derived growth potential in terms of jobs and revenues are discussed in Section 2 along the supply and value chain. Importantly, our project has a strong industrial involvement and is extremely well positioned for a successful post-project phase towards commercial use of the results through: a) Recycling process licensing, sales of equipment, control system, software b) Provision of environmental, safety, support to upscaling and validation services c) Packaging and composites solutions from recycled materials. Besides, our innovative new processes will also be enablers for innovation in other sectors that could adopt a similar approach or become end users of the new SRMs such as: packaging for additional applications that can turn circular at EoL such as pharma blisters, recycled thermoplastics for textile, injected goods e.g. for toys, tools or other consumer products, composite matrices and fibres used in the same but also combined with thermosetting matrices for transport in broader term, aeronautics, marine, construction, energy, sport goods, among many more. Below, we benchmark MultiCycle with existing solutions in terms of recycling and materials, all following growing trends: Packaging considered for recycling and circular use Following resource efficiency and lighweighting drivers, flexible packaging is growing in share vs. rigid counterparts especially thanks to improvements in properties. Flexible packaging represents ca. 43% of the plastic packaging market57, itself 40% of the plastic market. The amount of multilayer packaging films is increasing even in cases substituting rigid formats while saving materials. Multilayer packaging makes up about 14% of all flexible packaging films and 12% of the rigid packaging22. In general, plastic/plastic multilayer (ML) films can be subdivided into five different categories, whereby plastic/plastic are multilayer films with more than one plastic layer and a plastic content over 50% of the total mass (paper/plastic composites are excluded from the definition23). The market segments comprise: i) plastic/plastic ML without barrier layer, ii) plastic/plastic ML with organic barrier layer, iii) metallised plastic/plastic ML (films with coatings based on AlOx or SiOx,) iv) thermoformed (rigid) plastic/plastic ML, and v) plastic/plastic ML with Al foil (in decreasing market size), all relevant to MultiCycle. In Europe a total of about 1610 kt of multilayer films were distributed in 201623 corresponding to about 18,000 Mio m2 out of 130,000 Mio m2 of the total flexible packaging market23. In fact while our recycling process has unique advantages for dealing with multilayer packaging, it is in fact also relevant to the whole packaging films, including monolayers (conventional and bio-plastics), which are not currently recycled (so overall close to 10 Mio T per year5). As seen in the SOA (Section 1.4), there is no suitable industrial-scale recycling process available on the market to date leading to incineration with energy recovery, or landfilling in other countries, counteracting the efforts towards conservation of resources and crude oil-independency. The EU Circular Economy Package (CEP) sets clear goals for recycling 65% of municipal waste; and recycling 75% of packaging waste by 2030. As such our target fraction cannot continue to be kept out and innovations are clearly needed.  In terms of packaging materials currently produced from recycled materials, except from PET Bottle-to-Bottle, where the amount of r-PET is not marginal, use of pi-SRM, and low demanding packaging such as PE bottles for detergents, their presence is limited. This, besides generally observed properties being downgraded using established mechanical recycling processes is due to very stringent regulations when it comes to use in food contact. In food packaging, a recycled material can be incorporated into the centre layer; however recycled plastics from pc-waste should not be in direct contact with food according to Regulation (EU) No10/2011. For non-food packaging, no restriction exists concerning the contact with the filling material. No packaging from recycled material has typically reached the high amount of recycled plastics envisaged by MultiCycle. Further innovation potential will be generated through innovative converting (e.g. applying SiOx on recycled BO-PET) and smart ecodesigns that enable high SRM content in packaging even for food, as well as MultiCycling EoL. Composites considered for Recycling and circular use 
 
57 https://www.all4pack.com/content/location/136757, http://www.smitherspira.com/resources/2016/february/global-packaging-material-outlooks, http://www.smitherspira.com/resources/2016/february/global-packaging-material-outlooks

Although relevant to the whole fibre reinforced thermoplastic sector, due to its ability to maintain fibre length, MultiCycle is even more relevant to those with long fibres such as Long Fibre Thermoplastics (LFT) or nonwovens. The LFT market is estimated to grow from USD 3.28 Billion in 2016 to USD 5.55 Billion (480 thousand metric tons) by 2022, at a CAGR > 9% from 2017 to 202258. The applications of LFT include automotive, consumer goods, industrial goods, sporting goods, and others. The increasing use of LFT is driven by growing automobile production, regulatory requirements to improve fuel efficiency and the resulting replacement of steel with plastics. LFTs are being used in automotive applications for front-ends, bumper beams, dashboards, and underbody shields. They have a significant potential for mass-transit applications in buses, trucks, and railroad vehicles. LFTs are processed with a thermoplastic matrix, such as PP or PA reinforced with long glass (or carbon, aramid...) fibres with lengths >12 mm through a pultrusion processing method. LFTs can also be processed by injection or extrusion-compression moulding. Going one step further- since the longer the fibre, the better all main composite properties (fig 9)-, Bond-Laminates (BONDL) is the only current manufacturer that has (continuous fibres) weave-based thermoplastic composites (Trademark Tepex, commonly also referred to as organic sheets) in automotive mass production. Tepex® materials are manufactured made-to-order in a continuous lamination process. The manufacturing process, based on proprietary technology (patents listed in table 18), ensures complete consolidation of the individual fibre structure with the thermoplastic matrix (fig. 10). Being produced in a continuous process Tepex® materials can be delivered as sheets (with a standard width up to 1250 mm and a sheet length that can be optimized per application) or as cut to size sheets as well as complex geometric shapes ready for processing in thermoforming, compression or injection molding processes. ELG in turns has unique 100% r-CF and hybrid (eg. with PA) nonwoven (mats with r-CF length up to 100 mm) typically used in closed mould processes with liquid resins or converted by SCM. Reported applications are seat pans for example with hybrid nonwoven and bonnet parts with 100% rCF nonwoven. Besides the superior mechanical properties, the main advantage of all those thermoplastics composites in comparison with thermoset ones is that they are fully reformable and recyclable. Lower melting points than metals require less energy input to fabricate components and processing or reprocessing does not create any toxic effluents producing favourable LCA benefits. However, the current reprocessing technologies require the grinding and re-melting with a significant reduction of fibre length and in consequence a significant reduction of mechanical properties so recycling of composites is not closed-loop in terms of resource efficiency as recycled fibres or matrix cannot be reused in the same secondary applications59-60. The MultiCycle project aims to recycle these composite materials to be used in similar (semi-)structural applications as the virgin counterparts by maintaining the original length of the reinforcement fibre and properties of the polymeric matrix. The separated pure fractions will furthermore be compatible with a number of processes. Sheet Compression Moulding (SCM) is the most common choice for converting high-volume composite parts. This process produces high strength, complex parts in a wide variety of sizes. Features such as ribs, bosses and inserts can be moulded in. Compression moulded parts have 2 excellent finished surfaces and outstanding part-to-part repeatability. As seen in the state of the art, at the moment, in terms of composite recycling, practically only CF coming from pi sources are being recycled & re-used for the manufacturing of composites, the high cost of primary carbon fibres being the main driver. R-CF currently available on the market suffer a decrease in mechanical properties, but are 20 to 40 % cheaper than the primary material. Primary GF are relatively cheap and no high quality recycled glass fibres are currently on the market. Therefore, except the case of short r-GF included in a compound that is recycled via re-compounding or alike, the use of r-GF in composites is at the moment inexistent to the best of our knowledge. MultiCycle will deliver both high quality r-CF (to be channelled through ELG already producing r-CF) and r-GF (to be channelled through BONDL’s mother company LANXESS already producing virgin GF and compounds thereof) that can be recovered from selected pi and pc types at an acceptable cost when considering the overall recovery scheme of both polymer and fibres, therefore filling the currently existing gaps. Currently for BONDL, producing continuous fibre reinforced sheets at close to 50% fibres wt. ratio, there is no economically viable solution even for their pi wastes, which can be as much as 25% when cutting the final part. Indeed, even if mechanical recycling with high downgrading (going to short fibres for compounding) could be feasible, there is no recycler interested in such material as the dilution with pure material would have to be too significant and it is very abrasive for shredders. When filler fraction is too high or in presence of specific additives (eg. halogenated or when pc wastes contain

PVC), recycling is inhibited, and even incineration not feasible, leading to landfilling a lot of valuable materials (at a cost of 100-200€/T). Even if the project mainly tackles the challenge of fibre reinforced composites for the demonstration of the process and SRMs, the MultiCycle approach for ELV materials is also compatible for jointly recycling simpler fractions like neat polymers. Textiles (for use in composite structures) from recycled polymers Within the textile market, it is important to distinguish between pi and pc waste. Many textile extrusion companies re-use their own production waste directly in their process. The purity of these materials is very high and they have a perfect control of the input material. Very often this pi waste is mixed with virgin polymers at approx. 10% to avoid property decrease due to the multiple melting steps. Pc waste is much less valorised within the textile sector owing to the poor quality of the recycled polymers due to degradation and impurities (issues that could be compensated for by the MultiCycle approach), leading to uses in lower value applications (such as non-wovens for fleece fabrics and automotive low end applications). Moreover, the rheological and viscoelastic properties of the recycled materials induce some limitations for the application of the recycled products in the textile extrusion industry. The recovery of suitable material properties (i.e. Intrinsic Viscosity and molecular weight) relevant to the process parameters as well as aesthetic properties (i.e. whiteness; dyeability) by means of the application and/or introduction of selected chemicals can promote the usage of the recycled materials. Another main reason for the limited application of pc waste in textiles is the cost of recycled polymers which, in view of current respective properties, is too high compared to the virgin ones. This is predominantly because of the low availability of recycled polymers on the market unbalancing supply and demand leading to high price for recycled polymers and because of quality requirements (only high purity recycled materials such as PET from bottles can be used). Project partner SILON has vast experience with recycled raw material processing, mixing and blending to obtain a stable quality, e.g. using of end-reactors for liquid state polycondensation and can thus work with 100% recycled PET, from different higher and lower value sources.  Besides the mechanical recycling mentioned above, some companies also apply chemical recycling routes to recycle waste and re-use them in textile applications. The Italian company Aquafil collects PA post-consumer and post-industrial waste and depolymerises it to caprolactam monomers, which are next polymerised again to PA6 and processed to yarns for carpets and clothing. Compared to mechanical recycling, chemical recycling allows the removal of all foreign substances, resulting in a 100% virgin quality. However, it also implies several additional process steps, which increase cost very significantly. In contrast, MultiCycle obtains pure polymers and can supply the textile market with high quality recycled polymers, allowing the much broader usage of recycled materials, also in high value applications with strict quality requirements. As sustainability and the CE become increasingly important in the textile sector, thermoplastic fibres recovered from waste packaging or composite matrices hold a bright future, the properties of which will be mainly demonstrated in the demanding composite reinforcement application but are relevant to many more textile segments. To maximise our innovation commercial potential, along with the definition of the new value chains and outreach activities, a comprehensive series of measures including consumer perception, standardisation and regulatory aspects, as well as business planning, value proposition design, and knowledge /IPR management will be undertaken. While in recognition that it is important to Europe that future projects build on previously funded efforts and outputs in the spirit of open dissemination 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 28 of 117 
and sharing, MultiCycle’s intention is to open source a fair amount of the innovation and technology advancements. That said, MultiCycle also intends to file at least 2 patents, with a view to protecting the more strategic/valuable innovation within Europe. This, in turn, will secure freedom of use, exploitation and commercialisation for members of the consortium, as well as protecting competitive commercial advantages in Europe in these strategic areas. Hence, strategic IP will be anticipated and protected at the EU and US patent offices, and carefully managed in a way that will not impede the underlying aspirations to open source and make available the majority of MultiCycle’s innovation and technology to European researchers, SMEs, University and Industry to benefit from and to build on/from.  Taking into account existing patents from partners that will be used in the project as a basis for the planned advances in the project (see background table 13 in Section 2) and the 2 new ones that will be filled, we have a very strong IP position which will help to ensure our results stay under our control and benefit EU growth. The member base of the participating association in the consortium and AB (together representing over 200 recyclers, and 680 plastics and composites converters and 260 packaging & food producers) are all potential early adopters of the new recycling process or users of the SRMs (directly or through their value chain). Growing emphasis on Corporate Responsibility may indeed encourage companies currently not involved in recycling or plastic business, such as UNILEVER, which, as seen above, is building a recycling plant despite their core business is food and personal care. In fact, while the properties of the recovered polymers promise to be similar to the primary ones (as long as no significant deterioration occurred during first life, as is often the case for short term uses such as packaging- or which would be compensated for example by chain extenders), even plastic manufacturers like ARKEMA could be interested in hosting such a plant to recover pi- and maybe even selected pc- wastes. Already, freedom to operate has been given a major attention as it is vital for the commercial exploitation of the results. A patent search has been performed around each of the target technologies on which our new processes are based by the respective technology holders and experts (reflected as relevant in the state of the art). The prior art is indeed rich, as has been seen throughout Section 1.4. While certain areas are covered by patents- which will be taken into account in the project development-, there is certainly space for further innovation. Whenever possible, the project will consider not only developing confidential know-how, but also innovative aspects that could be protected by patents following a deepening of the IP search performed at proposal preparation stage and ascertaining the business opportunity- as is further explained in WP9 and Section 2.2. In particular we expect to derive IP from the CreaSolv® Process improvements and its smart monitoring and automation based on monitored data, as well as in the improvements enabling the recovery and reuse of the long fibres within composites, in addition to recycled matrix. 2. Impact 2.1 Expected impacts  
2.1.1. Expected impacts mentioned in the Work Programme.  
‘Plastics’ is one of the five priority areas addressed in the “EU action plan for the Circular Economy”62. MultiCycle has the ambition to become a key accelerator of the transition of the plastics industry to the CE and to be recognized as a pioneer through its Circular Plastic Helix. It is a high impact project to develop a sustainable, low carbon, resource 
 
61 excluding patents that deal with thermal recycling of FRP eg. milling and melting/ compounding as the resulting quality would not compete  62 COM/2015/0614 final, http://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/HTML/?uri=CELEX:52015DC0614&from=EN  
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 29 of 117 
efficient and competitive economy in Europe, as it is hinged on delivering a versatile process for recycling multi-materials and formulations based on SRMs to maximise the sustainability from cradle to cradle of a number of automotive composites and packaging applications. This impact will be achieved by demonstrating the commercial viability of the concept in practice, analysing the successful potential exploitation pathways and assessing and managing any related risks. MultiCycle is extremely well aligned with the the Plastic Strategy for Europe released on 16 January 2018 by the EC63 and it contributes significantly to the expected impacts (in blue below) listed in the topic CE-SPIRE-10-2018 Efficient recycling processes for plastic containing materials as described below:  More efficient & sustainable chemical process & processing technologies utilising plastic waste as starting material for the production of added value products such as recyclable plastic materials (e.g. composites) & chemicals excl. fuels As discussed in Section 1, MultiCycle is based on the patented CreaSolv® Process, which has already been tested by FRAUNHOFER on a wide number of materials from pi and pc origins including heterogeneous ones such as WEEE (whereby halogenated compounds were recovered as well as polymers), multilayer (where different polymers were recovered), composites (where matrix and fibres where shown to be recoverable), Building & Construction waste, etc. The flexibility of the process, so far tested up to TRL5 on complex materials and even TRL 7 on WEEE and for recovering mono-materials in laminated packaging, will be greatly improved in the delivered industrial pilot plant that will be capable of handling composites and packaging wastes. Process Analytical Technology (PAT) monitoring systems will enable the automation of the process and ensure constant quality of the output fractions, optimise the cycle time, etc. The PAT systems installed in the MultiCycle pilot plant will support the optimisation of yielded streams in spite of the variability of the input streams, thereby leading to improved efficiency. The composition of the waste materials will be checked using tailored monitoring systems for ELV and packaging wastes, as input data for optimally setting up the process on a waste batch to batch basis.  In the following process step, the reprocessing of such recovered polymer will also be optimised using sc-CO2 compounding where needed to compensate for any degradation occurred during primary use, and tailored reformulations to meet the SRM properties specified in WP1 for the different demonstrators (see Section 1.3) that we aim to produce in industrial processes in WP5 and 6 respectively for packaging and composites. Similarly we will pay particular attention to the recovered fibres to be reprocessed into long fibre composites or FRP, adding e.g. adequately selected compatibilizers in the compounds. An advantage of our process vs. others lies in the fact that plastic converters expect drop-in solutions and this is indeed granted by our recycled materials. There are no constraints, such as having to match the formulation of the primary use when planning the use for the SRM.  As seen in the State of the art regarding our core polymer recovery and reprocessing technologies, they have shown to preserve the quality and value of the virgin polymers. This is a great improvement since, in spite of collecting for recycling ca. 30% of all the plastic currently commercialised in EU, the EU plastic strategy deplores that this currently only retains 5% of its initial value64. Indeed, most of the recycling processes lead to significant downgrading of the properties. This is a key aspect that will be addressed by MultiCycle to provide a breakthrough vs. current state of the art recycling situation, especially when applied to currently generally landfilled or incinerated wastes. The added value products to be reprocessed from the SRM from the project are considered to be very similar to those of the primary ones: multilayer packaging and FRP (so high performance materials excluding any downgrading and lower value valorisation in the waste hierarchy pyramid- with exception of minor by-products amounts that could be used for energy recovery or used as fillers in basic value plastics, task 4.6).  In terms of comparison with other recycling processes- stressing once more that for the selected target materials to recycle, there is no efficient mature solution avoiding downgrading-, we can refer to recent results from the CloseWEEE project whereby CreaSolv® has been compared with other recovery technologies such Antisolv, Centrevap (lab scale) and Liquid Ion, as well as with mechanical recycling. It has been reported that from those technologies, CreaSolv® was the best performer with good upscaling ability. This situation is reflected in the Eco-efficiency diagram (fig 11). Detailed CAPEX/ OPEX are provided in the business plan, Section 2.2. It has also been demonstrated that CreaSolv® improves the sample cleaning (less impurities leading to better mechanical performance) and that the environmental impact was positive for all recycled grades.  The technologies proposed should provide a decreased utilisation of primary fossil resources in the process industry of at least 30%; The concepts proposed should provide a decrease in CO2 emissions of at least 20%;

While modern economies are highly dependent on plastics (at 99% from fossil resources) and Europe consumes in the range of 50 mio T per year5, only 25-30% of plastic waste in Europe is currently recycled (high variability between countries). In light of the EU’s CEP objective of achieving a resource efficient recycling society it is hard to accept that in Europe we still landfill 31% of plastic waste and 39% is incinerated65. This all contributes to the depletion of these limited resources.  To cope with this challenge, one of the current trends in the packaging industry is that of low-weighting to decrease resource consumption. Nevertheless, flexible packaging is more difficult to handle in mechanical recycling plants and the need of using multilayer barrier structures worsen the case as, for those, no market ready process is techno-economically feasible. Finding better solutions in the packaging sector is of utmost relevance as this sector consumes 40% of all plastic produced each year. Film recycling significantly contributes to the reduction of the Global Warming Potential in comparison to landfill as end of life option. According to the EC DG ENV "Plastic waste in the environment" study from 2011, plastic recycling could potentially reduce the GWP by 0.74 kg CO2 eq per kg recycled plastic vs. landfill66.  In addition, while bioplastics still have a very limited share among plastics (1%), they grow at a CAGR of over 10% and are surely a way to reduce the carbon footprint of involved primary resources. Indeed, by substituting fossil resources with bio-based raw materials, taking into account (bio)refining step, bioplastics can save 26 kg of CO2 equivalent per kg compared with conventional counterparts67, but also reduce climate change impact. 58% of produced bioplastics are used in packaging and 7% in automotive sector68 and are in both sectors seen as an emerging technology that drives future trends. The compatibility of the developed processes not only with currently more broadly used fossil based plastics, but also to maintain bioplastics value over several cycles in a more effective way than organic recycling and growing of new crops, is therefore an interesting added value in the preparation to the future Circular Bioeconomy for Europe, especially if bioplastics are used in multi-materials or complex/thick structures which in turn may jeopardize their compostability. In terms of automotive materials, every year, end-of-life vehicles (ELV) generate ca. 8 million tonnes of waste in the EU12, which should be managed correctly. Directive 2000/53/EC (ELV Directive) aims at making dismantling and recycling of ELVs more environmentally friendly. In line with the ELV, since 2015, manufacturers are due to employ materials that are reusable or recyclable in a share of over 95% in new cars and likewise to recover 85% of those from existing ELV. In cars, trends are also led by light weighting of materials used as it is recognized that reducing a car part by 100 kg leads lowering fuel consumption by 0.3L per 100 km so to CO2 emission reduced by 10 g per km. When considering that CO2 emissions originate predominantly from the use phase, with only ~1% being attributed to the recycling and waste stage15, substituting metals by structural composites such as Teplex seems extremely relevant. As such they will receive major focus in our demonstration and we will allow recyclability not to be a weakness anymore vs. metals in current ELV handling schemes. That will also help them replacing more widely used long fibres composites with thermosets matrices which recycling is still almost impossible. There are also calls for improved solutions for the growing share of thermoplastic composites which have additional materials such as metal inserts, paints, adhesives (all compatible with MultiCycle approach as opposed to mechanical recycling).  In terms of environmental impact, we can provide peer-reviewed LCA results from previous projects (TRL5-7) using CreaSolv® showing a benefit in terms of environmental impacts in all cases: e.g. EPS packaging study from BASF69, external study on WEEE70, etc. LCA data for the process itself are reported in table 7.

The main benefits are related not only to the reduced CO2 footprint, but also to the possibility of closing the loop through viable recycling reducing the pressure in terms of exploitation of natural/non-renewable resources. LCA results from using CreaSolv® showed a benefit in terms of CO2 emissions reduction ranging from 33% (1.2 kg CO2 eq.) to 99% (3.4 E+03 kg CO2 eq.) compared to conventional multilayer packaging and composites treatments (Table 5). Production upscaling is expected to lead to further improvements in terms of environmental impact.  The energy consumption is estimated to be reduced up to 70-90% for the production of recycled composites vs the energy necessary for the primary production of composites49, besides avoiding emissions of particles and various VOCs emitted during inefficient incineration (when not prohibited by the presence of e.g. PVC in the mixed light fraction from ELV leading to landfill as currently deplored by GBP). It was in fact estimated that plastics production and the

incineration of plastic waste give rise globally to approximately 400 million tonnes of CO2 a year63. Seeing such figures and bearing in mind the target using of >70% of recycled materials in all our secondary applications (see next paragraph), thereby substituting primary (fossil or bio-based) resources by SRM, there is no doubt that we can on the long term expect an overall significant effect on the plastic processing industry (especially as our 2 end uses consume 50% of all plastic produced). Importantly, we will design our demonstrators so they can virtually be recycled infinitely with the same recycling approach. All in all, the use of renewable (recyclable and recycled) resources in our target sectors yields countless advantages that can be used by brand owners to support their green image, corporate responsibility, such as reduced environmental impact, increased recycling of own pi- waste to improve sustainability, lowered greenhouse gases emissions, while safeguarding the performances of SRM in a cost competitive way. By delivering our process and resulting RMs and products to the market, the overall environmental impact will be decreased along the new value chains as will be justified by LCA, LCC and S-LCA during the project and we will also indirectly address a huge number of very relevant other environmental and socially important challenges as discussed later on. The concept should utilise at least 70% of waste material including at least 40% of plastic waste; As seen in Section 1.3, the used polymer recovery process is very versatile, and can adapt to different qualities and compositions of raw materials including the most challenging ones targeted in the project such as multilayer packaging and composites that respectively contain additivated plastics of many types, adhesives, inks, metals, paper and formulated plastics matrix with particles, plasticizers, fibres, paints, inserts, etc. The recovery of each substance is generally feasible by adding further steps with tailored solvent and conditions in the MultiCycle process (fig. 2) but the economic feasibility of the recovery of a minor fraction has to be ascertained on a case by case basis (as will be tested in WP2 at small technical scale, WP4 at large pilot scale and WP7 in terms of final global TEA). In any case in the target waste fractions (fig 12 and 13), we will always recover above 80% (main layers in multilayer plastics, and matrix, fibres, + optionally fillers, in automotive composites with a content above 40% of plastics even in composites with high amount of fillers and/or fibres). Our target applications are generally similar to the primary ones, which therefore show that while we will not downgrade the single materials performance, we can expect a similar fraction of recycled materials to be usable as the one recovered. All the demonstrators have already been carefully thought of to meet such target and mainly the additives and minor fractions (e.g. thin barrier layer) not recycled will be tapping upon virgin resources. Such target is also feasible for food packaging although the sealing layer will be limited to SRM from pi waste, but all other main layer can be from pc waste- SRM. For such high recycled plastic amounts (far above the state of the art) to actually become used in marketable products beyond mere product demonstrators, in addition to our targeted dissemination activities discussed later, our circularity will help to mobilise both ends of the value chains. Indeed, at the one end we have partners that want to provide their pi waste- or that the pc they are responsible for is recycled to avoid landfill taxes- and at the other end, we have partners that want to reuse this material as a less expensive alternatives to conventional materials and with better environmental credentials.  Effective dissemination of major innovation outcomes to the current next generation of employees of the SPIRE sectors, through the development, by education/training experts, of learning resources with flexible usability. These should be ready to be easily integrated in existing curricula and modules for undergraduate level and lifelong learning programmes. Activities purely aimed at disseminating the project results are broadly covered in Section 2.2. In addition, key attention has been paid to embed Effective knowledge transfer from the technical partners in the project to organisations both internal and external to the consortium, as is addressed in Task 8.1 Education, training and capacity building. This activity is fundamental for paving the way towards commercialization, guaranteeing that sufficient knowledge and competencies to

The involvement of the SME BRITEST, who is a SPIRE member and well networked within SPIRE (and will ensure effective targeting of the SPIRE Sectors), and who has a track record in working closely with and in transferring research results to industry, as well as providing training and educational programmes, is a valuable asset, which will ensure that we bridge the innovation gap that frequently prevents industry adoption of R&D outputs. In addition to their expertise in supporting the transfer of innovative research into industrial uptake through a variety of scalable training mechanisms, BRITEST led the SusChem-funded Educate to Innovate (E2I) project that explored how EU funded research could be used to enhance academic courses to support the training of the next generation of industrial practitioners. E2I demonstrated that, whilst it was not possible to develop a single module based on an innovative output that would meet the needs of university courses across Europe, it is possible for IA projects to provide materials and case studies that can be tailored by academics to fit within their own institutions’ requirements. The training activity within MultiCycle will draw on the experience of E2I, further enhanced by guidance developed within the H2020-SPIRE SPRING project and its Academic Stakeholder Group, to develop materials and case studies that will enable the academic community to exploit the results of MultiCycle to enhance their educational offer with the novel concepts and system design principles that will be identified and developed during the project. Some of the topics tackled in the project shall become part of Master degree courses, as well as research topics for Master & PhD theses, offering students a taste of research problems that have practical impact and interest, and the opportunity to perform research in a high quality international environment. To maximise the use and impact of the project outputs, a MultiCycle Capacity Building Programme will be designed and delivered through Task 8.1 to support stakeholders across Europe. This programme will address: 1. Infrastructure sustainability, intending to facilitate the adoption of the MultiCycle process. 2. Human capital development, by offering: (i) Training programs (e-training modules, online modules and online workshops) on the functionalities, configuration, operation and use of the MultiCycle pilot plant and of the resulting SRM, and (ii) Collaboration tools (communication and knowledge sharing tools integrated in the e-training modules) that will facilitate the flow of knowledge and experience (including best practices) between involved stakeholders. 3. Organisational development, aiming at supporting continuous learning and incorporation of lessons learned into future materials and update of best practices. The MultiCycle Capability Building Programme will aim at keeping participants’ knowledge and capabilities sharp during and after the project. Therefore, activities include:  Agreeing on the design and management of training activities.  Developing “dynamic” flexible and modular formats capable of being continuously updated.  Setting up online workshops and hands-on training labs, to allow experimentation and piloting with the MultiCycle pilot plant and SRM test use cases (WP4, 5, 6 mainly), amplifying the opportunities to learn, develop & apply the new skills.  Creating a trusted community of knowledge exchange via the Circular Plastics Helix to encourage the interactive sharing of knowledge and best practices between participants. BRITEST will ensure effective targeting of SPIRE sectors.  2.1.2. Other substantial impacts (Socio-Economic & Environmental) Besides addressing unmet demands for recycling multi-materials in controlled waste management context and therefore creating growth in Europe, MultiCycle will also contribute to other environmental and socially important impacts especially in terms of pollution and consumer health that are side effects but equally important in the EU plastic strategy. According to a report of the Ellen MacArthur foundation2, the amount of plastic wastes leaking from our waste management streams shows as much as 32% of plastic ending up in our environment (fig. 14). Long term plastic persistence in the environment is aggravated by a cumulative effect, due to both accidental and intentional littering. Recognized as one of the major international environmental challenges by the European Environment Agency (EEA)71, plastics account for 89-95% of marine litter (roughly 8 million tons every year). Besides, 25 MT of plastic waste is accumulated annually in soil worldwide, being detrimental to the environment (irreversible soil contamination, degradation of soil quality, pollution of rivers and groundwater) and human health (heavy metals, dioxins and furans released during plastic burning are associated with endocrine disruption, heart disease, cognitive 
 
71 State of Europe's seas, EEA Report No 2/2015, ISSN 1977-8449 
Fig. 14: Global flows of plastic materials in 2013 (Ellen Mc Arthur Fundation) 

                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 33 of 117 
and motor disabilities, and human carcinogen)72.  MultiCycle solutions for improved economic viability of plastic recycling & reduced landfilling will contribute to lowering the amount of plastics leaking out from controlled waste management systems resulting in reduced environmental pollution and improved ecosystems and a positive contribution in relation to consumer access to healthier soils (reduced landfill) and seas and therefore food that contains less harmful substances leaking from plastics or microplastic fragments. Contribution to improving the innovation capacity and the integration of new knowledge According to estimates of the EU plastic strategy, only 5% of the value of plastic packaging material retains in the economy, the rest is lost after a very short first-use73. As such, the MultiCycle innovation will allow the EU to reduce the related annual bill of between €70 and €105 billion lost by not maintaining plastic within Circular Economy at sufficient value. In terms of the automotive industry alone, we estimate this to cost directly > €16 billion (5Mio T per year of plastic used in automotive sector at an average cost of 3€/kg+ landfill costs). While the generation of new knowledge is positive, it is in its industrial use that innovation capacity is increased and impact is realised. As such, to allow generating truly market replicable innovation, the MultiCycle consortium unites the industry value chain necessary for market success, and the project has integrated a number of transversal activities such as environmental, regulatory, safety and techno-economic assessments, and finally business planning activities to maximise the post-project exploitation of the results. The project gives trans-sectorial industry and transdisciplinary research and technology players an opportunity to collaborate and share all required technical knowledge and market expertise. Effective knowledge transfer from the RTDs (AIMPLAS, FRAUNHOFER, CTB) to the participating enterprises, especially SMEs often lacking own R&D capacities, laying down the basis for supporting the industry partners to carry forward the results post-project, is fundamental to increasing the likelihood of market uptake. To this end, a dedicated task, T8.1 Education, training and capacity building, with key focus on SPIRE sectors, will ensure seamless knowledge transfer occurs.  As discussed in the innovation potential section, MultiCycle is founded on a unique patented process (with potential patentable improvements), which together with the high added value applications, have great potentials vs. existing processes and recycled products. We have included in the work plan all activities to maximise the post project readiness of the process and its PAT system to exploit the project results in a short term and therefore grant the availability of SRM of high quality to feed the downstream value chains. To increase the innovation capacity of the industry, our approach is to capitalise on a valuable opportunity for the EU plastic recycling industry and the end-user industries to substitute currently used virgin, often fossil based, materials. The closed loop will also provide them with opportunity to obtain value from their waste instead of having to pay for their disposal. MultiCycle represents a very timely development to enable circular shift in the EU plastic industry (which is a major industry within the European Process Industries and a key SPIRE sector). This will result in great business opportunities reinforced by increased visibility, and policy awareness and support for such solutions. Following the current market trends for recycling, the respective growth in the target end use sectors that we will tap into and in view of the massive EU plastic business (€350 billion turnover per annum), MultiCycle will be a key enabler to support the CEP targets of recycling 75% of plastics by 2030, leading to significant revenues and jobs at the waste provision upstream and recycling levels of the value chain. They will be reinforced at the downstream (SRM converters & end users) level who benefit from new products, revenues from their pi wastes and reduced landfilling taxes. Estimates about how partners respectively expect to grow their business and create jobs with the new MultiCycle solutions are given in section 2.2. MultiCycle offers the European Plastics Process Industry a concrete and economically sound way to get a strong foothold in the multimaterial recycling, which will lead to an invaluable headstart in this global competition.  2.1.3 Main Barriers/obstacles/framework conditions We have already identified a number of framework conditions and during WP9, we aim at using several tools like SWOT (draft of which can be found in Section 2.2) and Porters’ Five Forces analyses to identify and manage the relevant barriers and threats that could impact our technical and business success.

Value chains readiness- The actual success of new recycling technologies like MultiCycle depends on the willingness of the full value chain to embrace it. As such, we will prepare for the future creation of operative new value chains based on extensively tested and demonstrated new recycling process and SRM solutions. As further justified in Section 3.4, the consortium leverages partners at every stage of the supply and value chain, as well as additional companies and sectoral associations in the AB. To support the market deployment of the project results, we integrate in as much as possible with related existing upstream value chains in terms of waste sourcing by: i) maximising compatibility with waste management systems in model EU countries will be the object of task 1.1 by ISWA, ii) we expect to be able to directly plug and play our new process as a solution for recycling currently non recycled fractions within the generally already well organised collection systems for EoL packaging, iii) automotive companies can handle the new process through dismantlers that can feed new MultiCycle recyclers (specialised for composites, or multifeedstocks) or using it directly in the recovery of ELV plastic parts or post industrial wastes. In terms of use of SRM from MultiCycle process, again we maximise the compatibility with existing downstream chain where: i) the polymers and fibres can be uptaken by established compounders (specialised or not in recycled plastics as we deliver polymers with no downgrading) who may need minor process adjustment such as sc-CO2 inlet, ii) Reprocessing of SRM with similar technologies as virgin materials and generally similar to primary applications to provide pull and push effects for the circular value chain to be successful. This closed loop scenario maximising synergies along the value chain will be tested in the project. However, it is not considered as a real limitation since pure polymers & fibres will be obtained and thus will be available for any application that can benefit from our results (later discussed fig. 20). As illustrated in Fig. 16, in MultiCycle, we aim to create 2 new stable and effective circular value chains (with possible interconnections) that can supply waste and substitute their petrol-derived virgin products with recycled polymers and fibres. The value chain approach, furthermore supported by systematic training programme, will also guarantee that sharing of selected knowledge occurs among the participating partners to reach a seamless exploitation tapping into increasing market and consumer demand for environmentally friendly materials but also on the regulatory obligations supporting the CE transition. Cooperation among MultiCycle members will be particularly important in the post-project phase, as results of the project are pre-competitive and additional investment before MultiCycle is ready for commercialisation is required (and will require engagements across the value chain).  Recovering useful resources/energy out of disposed products or by-products (originating from the EU plastics processing industry) leads to new CE business models to be studied in task 9.4. A DST will be deployed to maximize the value extracted from the project results evaluating the environmental and economic feasibility of circularity in a full set of paths. Legislation & policies- MultiCycle will be a key enabler for implementing the Circular Economy Package (CEP), the Waste Framework Directive (WFD), the Packaging and Packaging Waste Directive (PPWD), the New German Packaging Law replacing the current Packaging Ordinance by 201974, ELV, and other frameworks pertinent to our innovations. Regulations and standards can represent a massive ally for the implementation of new technologies related with the Circular Economy. As such the project includes policy makers outreach efforts in WP9 to demonstrate how the new technical solutions could lead to significant increase in recycling rates and how they could benefit in their market introduction from public incentives and support along the value chains.  Nevertheless, current legislations can also result in barriers and this is especially the case in terms of use of recycled materials for food contact that is currently generally not allowed with few exceptions such as PET from bottle to bottle as fruits and vegetables for which the use of recycled PE have already been approved75. Although we consider alternative applications too (textiles for composites, secondary packaging, food packaging with a functional virgin or pc waste based food contact layer), we will benefit from FRAUNHOFER’s unique experience in such matters at EU level to certify the technical usability of the recovered materials in food contact. In view of the existing result on the purity delivered by CreaSolv®, we are confident that test results will demonstrate usability and such data will be used in policy outreach so that

our SRM are food contact approved to maximise their market potential. Whereas our main target for recovery in terms of polymers and fibres are exempt from registration and evaluation under REACH (EC No 1907/2006), any other additives recovered will be checked to prove that its structure is equal to the virgin ones maintaining its REACH status. Furthermore, all regulations needed to obtain the CE marks for the MultiCycle pilot plant will be followed to enable its safe operation in ATEX environments (handling solvents) in Task 4.2. Others- As other boundary conditions, awareness about the right sorting of the new materials and end of life treatment will be required to foster best practices among citizens, waste management bodies, recyclers, etc. Likewise, the perception of underperformance of any recycled materials or even related safety concerns will have to be handled. This will be greatly facilitated by our dissemination and exploitation activities, which will be continued in post-project efforts to create adequate policies, demonstrate economic viability, and encourage intra and inter value chain cooperation.  Possible barriers may also arise from the need for specific knowledge of how to perform the MultiCycle process especially at the CreaSolv® step. We have therefore included extensive training and knowledge sharing actions in the workplan for targeted at the process industries under the leadership of BRITEST. Moreover, the advanced & user friendly PAT control systems that will be implemented will facilitate process understanding and implementation.  As previously discussed, the required investments to implement a MultiCycle process will also be considered as a potential barrier and therefore we will favour a synergistic approach compatible with existing infrastructures for all the other steps (that are very little changed) and where waste generators could also be secondary users and possibly host a recycling unit for the pi, and collected pc wastes. For fostering a green economy, growth in income and employment are often driven by public and private investments to reduce carbon emissions and pollution, enhance energy and resource efficiency, and prevent the current resource depletion. These investments need to be catalysed and supported by targeted public expenditure, policy reforms and regulation changes. To this end, a key barrier that could prevent us from achieving our expected impacts is the lack of a favourable enabling framework and regulatory conditions for further incentivising the shift to a Circular Plastic Economy. Consequently, engagement with policy makers, considering possible public incentives to support initial ramp up of the new recycling facilities by factoring socio economic benefits gained over the whole value chain, is an important aspect of this project that will be supported by the implication of ISWA in the consortium. MultiCycle will without doubt benefit from the CE plastic strategy and its enforcement throughout EU, as well as contribute to it whereby it will engage to propose a concrete set of actions. An important measure that MultiCycle will support lies in the alignment of the fees for waste management of marketed materials to their environmental credential. We propose for example, the collection and landfill to be transferred to the recycler of the multi-materials using novel processes such as MultiCycle (as a governmental incentive that could even be financed by increasing fees on the materials with greatest environmental burden) to maximise the introduction of our SRM which would become, in addition to their environmental unique selling points at equal performance, cheaper than virgin counterparts. Given that about 80% of European customers want to buy products with a minimal impact on the environment76, our materials could indeed succeed even without such incentives, as we respond to these consumer needs. As discussed in section 2.2, we will also look at other public and private funding to support this market replication step as the initial investment or other public incentives will be key in transitioning the recycling companies in engaging in the novel recycling process across the Valley of Death.
2.2 Measures to maximise impact 
2.2.1 Plan for the dissemination and exploitation of the project results. Our vision is to lead the transition towards a sustainable post-petroleum society in the plastic sector- under the major SPIRE sector linked to Chemicals- while decoupling economic growth from resource depletion and environmental impact by serving industry with the provision of cost-effective industrial plastics recycling processes that are economically and environmentally sustainable especially focusing in currently non-recyclable multi-materials. In order to assist Europe in the transition towards a Circular Economy, new sustainable cross-sectorial value chains will be established providing competitive recycled polymer feedstocks with properties comparable to virgin polymers, thus reducing reliance on new fossil-based resources. In order to realise this vision, post-project exploitation pathways have been carefully planned already during MultiCycle proposal preparation. We have devised our initial strategy for the dissemination and the exploitation of the project results, with a view to increasing the innovation and business outcome from the project as well as its social and environmental impacts. Outline of the dissemination plan: The dissemination & communication plan is illustrated in fig. 17, further detailed in table 20 at the end of Section 2, and comprises a set of actions to transfer the non-confidential results and knowledge generated during the project to ensure that as many relevant audiences as possible have access to them. The dissemination plan for MultiCycle will be led and facilitated by WP8 leader BRITEST – an organisation with extensive experience in managing the dissemination of results from multi-disciplinary, collaborative innovation projects and one that is well positioned to outreach and engage with the European Process Industries – and supported through the co-operative 
 
76 Eurobarometer survey, European Commission, 2013 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 36 of 117 
work of the consortium partners. Dissemination activities will be reviewed, evaluated and refined throughout the project to ensure the effectiveness of each individual action. Our underpinning purposes are to heighten awareness about the tangible and intangible outputs of the MultiCycle IA, to make these outputs available to those best placed to make use of them, and to broaden their impact beyond the original project focus. In this way we aim to avoid duplication of research, and contribute to knowledge building and advancement of innovation in Europe. There is a strong mutuality between dissemination of project results and communications activities on behalf of the project (described more fully later). We will employ both traditional channels such as conferences (presentations, papers and posters), publications in peer-reviewed and open access journals, dissemination in industry magazines, newsletters and on-line platforms, as well as more vanguard channels, such as social media platforms (ResearchGate, Academia.edu, SlideShare, Twitter, YouTube, LinkedIn), blogs, etc. The project website, will serve as a lasting virtual hub for disseminating downloadable public deliverables and outputs of MultiCycle, as it will house the ‘MultiCycle Green Channel’ for open access to the peer-reviewed publications of the project.  All in all, the primary platform from which these various actions will be co-ordinated will be a new Circular Plastics Helix (virtual technology cluster) to be established by V2020. This will act as a vehicle to attract additional stakeholders (e.g. plastic packaging, product and component manufacturers and users, recyclers, processing and recycling equipment vendors, local waste management authorities, regulatory bodies, policy makers, researchers, academia, etc.) and through which to seek and address answers to ongoing questions, concerns, issues or challenges that may arise. The Helix will establish an online community promoting: a) visibility to end-users of the project and its bigger picture sustainability context for the EU plastics industry, b) a sense of both inclusion and solidarity by virtue of being united with a broader group who are collectively pursuing a shared and common vision, c) a vehicle to regularly post updates, progress reports, news and events to the stakeholders involved, maintaining and sustaining their interest, (in that sense it will be cross-linked with the project website and share contents of all MultiCycle’s main news, blog post, etc.), d) a central online space for the project, restricted to members and contributors to the project, allowing users to post questions and receive answers in an open and transparent forum.  V2020 is a unique open innovation platform that connects RTOs and businesses to collaborate, innovate and grow in the area of research and innovation. The network currently consists of more than 350 organisations from 42 countries (including membership from all EU Member States) demonstrating pan European reach and impact to ca. 400k researchers and innovators. The V2020 Helix model (Fig. 18) creates virtual technology clusters that are generally led by RTOs (here AIMPLAS, a recognized RTO in the field of CE for plastics and in view of their huge network of affiliated companies). There are currently 16 live Helixes in areas such as Materials, Manufacturing, etc). The Circular Plastics Helix will be integrated into the V2020 Helix network and will be promoted at all relevant V2020 events across Europe (ca. 12 thematic collaboration events per year). The Helix will use V2020’s technology tool “Crowdhelix” so that project partners and all relevant stakeholders from the V2020 network can profile their expertise and interest in MultiCycle activities. All dissemination activities will be developed with an eye towards the exploitation of the project results by other parties, to lay down the basis for subsequent innovation actions as well as other private initiatives which can accelerate the route towards the take-up of new technologies within the MultiCycle selected applications of plastics recycling and converting and beyond. 
2.2.2 Initial Business & Exploitation for MultiCycle A.   Market Analysis & Segmentation:  Plastic Recycling and recycled plastic market: The global plastic recycling market has been gaining a steady momentum over the past few years due to the related directives and growing awareness about CO2 emissions and the need to reduce them. This, together with the overflowing state of landfills has also triggered an urgent need for recycling plastic instead of producing new material. According to recent market reports, the global plastic recycling market, which was valued at €25 bn in 2015 (of which only €2 Bn in EU10 ) is expected to reach a figure of €45 bn by 2024. During the forecast period of 2016 and 2024, the global market is expected to grow at a CAGR of 6.9%. Analysts anticipate that China alone will make a contribution of over 50% to the global plastic recycling market. Europe is also expected to show steady growth in the global market due to stringent laws pertaining to the use of plastic in the region. Furthermore, the increasing awareness about protection of the environment is also anticipated to boost the Europe plastic recycling market. In 2012, out of the 25.2Mt of plastics collected for recycling, 6.6 Mt were recycled and 8.9 Mt were incinerated. Incineration is massively used, with for example 38.8% of plastic packaging waste5 treated that way, which as seen, contributes to global warming and recovers insufficient value. Currently, according to the Plastic Recyclers Europe, 1000 companies are operating with 30.000 employees and a 3Mt installed capacity10. Thus, the European plastic recycling market is not only saturated, but there is a huge potential for adopting new efficient technologies if they are proved economical viable. In terms of material, the global plastic recycling market is segmented into PET, PP, HDPE, LDPE, PS, PVC, ABS, PA and PC. These types of plastics are used among others for making bottles for household chemicals, storage containers, and personal care products. The global competitive landscape of the recycling market is quite fragmented in terms of key players (including e.g. Kuusakoski Oy, Plasgran Ltd., Envisison Plastics, KW plastics etc.77).  
Cost of recycling: The disposal of the recyclable waste in landfills compared to recycling appears at first glance cheaper because no sorting or processing is required beyond transporting the waste to the landfill, it requires less human labour cost and less energy for work done by machines. But in reality, recycling is not always the cheapest and most efficient alternative thus the reuse becomes an obvious option where applicable. Reuse, recycling (including organic recycling where applicable), energy recovery and even landfills have materials for which they are the least wasteful disposal method, but as technology finds new ways to sort and recycle waste, the fraction of waste going to landfills or incineration can certainly decrease. More specifically, the following cost are taken into account for recycling:  Energy Costs: expressed as the ratio between the energy cost to recycle the product into SRM and the energy to make products from virgin materials: there are energy savings when manufacturing from recycled rather than virgin material for all the typical recyclable materials (in the range of 70-90% less for MultiCycle vs. primary source, as seen in section 2.1).  Transportation Costs: Transporting materials to recycling facilities, the fuel used to transport recyclable fractions 
 
77 https://www.transparencymarketresearch.com/plastic-recycling-market.html 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 38 of 117 
is a significant cost that can range from €16 to €57 per ton77. The logistics costs will be studied in MultiCycle depending on the uptake of the new process which may be at an established recycler having a non-recyclable fraction from its own sorting (therefore not adding transport), or a different actor in the supply and value chain requiring a different collection approach.  Sorting and Handling Costs: Another significant cost is the time and attention it takes to properly sort recycled materials at the collection facility. In the current recycling industry, the various plastics need to be separated by their resin type excluding complex materials. This laborious step will not be necessary for MultiCycle, where mixed fractions are accepted. Instead of a sorting system, we will use composition monitoring data as input to set up the MultiCycle process.  Value as Commodities: Recycled plastics are commodities and thus they have value in the marketplace that can help covering the cost of recycling. The price of commodities goes up and down depending on supply, demand and the political climate. Current commodities prices for recycled materials are available for purchase through several distributors.78 According to EPA, a well-run curbside recycling program can cost anywhere from €40 to more than €122 per ton. Trash collection and disposal programs, on the other hand, cost from €57 to more than €160 per ton. This demonstrates that, while there is still room for improvements, recycling can be cost-effective and, if managed correctly, should cost cities (and taxpayers) less than garbage disposal for any given equivalent amount of material.79 For example, a study taking into account the macro-level costs and savings of their recycling programs showed it costs close to €15 M more per year than landfill disposal, but allows savings of €20–100 M per year80. With a well-designed program and the right technology, recycling is therefore efficient in terms of money, energy and natural resources when compared to a linear system that manufactures everything from virgin materials and sends it all to landfills when consumers discard it. 
B. Identification of Exploitation Plans, Key Exploitable Results (KERs) & Routes for Exploitation.  The MultiCycle consortium mobilises the necessary partners for the pilot scale development of the different MultiCycle recycling and reprocessing steps (WP2-3), its demonstration at a precompetitive scale (WP4) but also to validate the new recycled plastics applications for packaging and automotive (WP5-6) as environmental and economic sustainability (WP7), a set of activities which are all highly relevant for future market exploitation (WP9) that will be strongly supported by the dissemination and training activities (WP8). A market-oriented approach will drive MultiCycle IA capitalising on the expertise of the recyclers, plastic converters and end users in the consortium and AB, ensuring that sufficient knowledge is collated to steer the research towards delivering a solution with good market potential. Our intention is to realize all exploitation pathways for the results:  a) Commercial exploitation: a key exploitation route will be the industrial use of the project knowledge for the creation of innovative recycling processes resulting in a broad range of SRMs with true market potential and virtually no property downgrading, uses within and beyond the validated applications, new monitoring systems, engineering and advisory services to deliver high competitiveness and growth impacts.  b) Education and training: the use of the new knowledge generated for upskilling and enriching curricula in European education will have a high impact for raising the European knowledge base to prepare current and future generations of plastics recyclers, producers and end users in multiple sectors of the European Process Industry, as well as discrete manufacturing for the transition to the Circular Economy;  c) Further research: planning future spin-off research and development projects especially in areas that will contribute to the transferability of the project results to new applications, with particular focus on ensuring enduring impact in the European Process Industries and SPIRE sectors;  d) Contribution to standards: while providing techno-economically feasible and sustainable solutions to enable the recycling of materials which currently only have deficient end of life options, the project results could on the long term contribute towards new regulations in the field of plastic waste management among others (see section 2.1.3). During the proposal stage, each partner has communicated clear exploitation expectations (Table 8), in line with business activity and positioning on the value chain. The Exploitation Plans will be drawn up in detail in WP9 to maximize the innovation impact. While we have favoured the involvement of mainly SMEs along the value chain of the new recycling process implementation (IRIS, LOEMI, VERTECH, AXIA) for whom a successful market uptake of the results can provide a significant growth opportunity for their businesses, the large companies involved as material suppliers/converters and end users (ARKEMA, BONDL, AMCOR, FARPLAS, SILON, ELG, TOFAS) have in turn a massive potential to act as powerful market pull and push actors, which will lead to substantial growth and significant impact for the EU. As early adopters, these blue chip enterprises will act as lighthouses for other actors in related sectors.

Further opportunities to manage MulltiCycle’s IP towards the market and commercialisation through the partners’ planned go-to-market efforts. Protecting strategic IP will secure freedom of use, exploitation & commercialisation and competitive advantages in EU in these strategic areas.

19 ISWA Waste management  Support members of ISWA to access new technologies to recycle currently non recyclable materials and influencing the evolution of waste policy to promote recycling

MultiCycle Business Model Canvas  MultiCycle partners have already set up preliminary business plans, which will be further studied and elaborated during the project. For simplicity reasons, the Business Model Canvas has been used and crosses the entire supply chain of MultiCycle, featuring core KERs, starting by the development of MultiCycle processing plants and finishing at the polymer producers of SRMs (Table 10). Each partner with established sales network and distribution channels will approach the 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 41 of 117 
relative market before the extension of the commercialization. Thus, for each involved partner it will be necessary to identify the total number of potential customers based on the current trends of the market. Once the target market is established, the analysis of the competitors, when present, will allow the partners to make a sales forecast, based on their market share. Finally, a product commercialization phase will be planned to turn the project’s components into a suite of commercially viable products and services. During the project, each partner will proceed with the development of an individualized business plan, under the activities of WP9. This will be instrumental to develop and pivot the related business models during the project. The final business plans (D9.4) will provide the details on how the project’s results will be used in commercial exploitation activities, for training purposes, for further research activities as well as for standardisation activities. They will also specify in detail for each result both joint & individual exploitation strategies in terms of technology roadmaps, finance requirements, commercial opportunities and related business plans, taking into account IPR. Further measures will be planned in the framework of this task to increase the likelihood of continuity of the established ecosystem and value chains, as well as market uptake of MultiCycle KERs for European industrial sustainability, competitiveness, growth and jobs.  Our new process and products will present technical and commercial advantages which the industrial and academic partners of the MultiCycle consortium will exploit during and after the project. The entire approach towards the novel circular recycling and reuse solutions deployed in the MultiCycle will present unique branding properties with high market potential compared to the existing solutions where fossil-based polymers are often landfilled or incinerated. The reduction of carbon footprint towards the entire life cycle of the proposed materials is an additional advantage of the MultiCycle solution.
