F. Market deployment plan of main MultiCycle KERs Fig 11 represents the expected full scale coordinated deployment of the different steps of the MultiCycle process and resulting semi-finished materials and finished products. We expect the process to reach TRL 9 and start sales 2 to 3 years post-project, allowing SRMs to be delivered in our target markets from the next year and the adoption to new applications in the following years. Although MultiCycle requires a substantial investment into a new recycling process capability, its high compatibility/complementarity with existing waste management streams and the critical mass of materials available throughout Europe to be thus recycled can accelerate the market introduction of the MultiCycle results. In addition, the subsequent reprocessing and secondary materials’ applications, often in closed loop, were chosen to require limited to no investments. As such, only process fine-tuning is required and should not delay the use of the MultiCycle materials once recycled polymers and fibres become available. Once the initial application of MultiCycle in the automotive and packaging sectors successfully reach the market, further momentum will be created around the replication to other sectors both as suppliers of end of life materials (especially composites) and as users of the secondary materials (whereby uses within numbers SPIRE sectors are envisaged, all enabled by the engineering sector- through IRIS & LOEMI- being core in SPIRE). In terms of the measures that will be in place in order to effectively plan for the exploitation of the results of the project, the robust industry involvement in the consortium (SMEs and large companies, including leaders in the polymers and composite fields), backed by strong knowledge management, knowledge transfer and market exploitation plans, and 

                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 42 of 117 
their key role in the project activities will boost an industry-driven approach to the project respecting market, consumer and regulatory requirements. This will ensure that MultiCycle is successful in transitioning knowledge towards the market overcoming potential barriers that may hinder the swift uptake of the results of MultiCycle research in the post-project phase.  The very strong training as well as market & technical knowledge transfer programme led by BRITEST, described in section 2.1.1 and to be implemented in WP8, will be a key enabler for the innovation to transfer smoothly among partners along the value chains and TRL levels. The inclusion of 2 companies (AXIA, ILS) with extensive experience in translating R&D output into actual commercialisation and market penetration, is another important added value for the consortium and will contribute to ensuring a systematic and methodological approach to planning for the important post-project stage. MultiCycle consortium furthermore includes ISWA, which has direct links and communications with numerous public and private entities working worldwide in the field of waste management and recycling worldwide, a lot of which could be potential licensees for the new recycling process across Europe.  Importantly, we will follow a cautious and transparent communication policy both in terms of sourcing and end of life, backed up by LCA results, which will contribute to raising industry and policy makers confidence that will further support the market-introduction of environmental-friendly processes, as well as the new sustainable packaging and automotive materials.  Sound management of the knowledge generated during the project, active planning in relation to post-project exploitation activities, further work for escalating along the TRLs, as well as scouting for possible viable financing routes to commercialisation that will allow us to quickly scale up, bridge the innovation gap, navigate the ‘valley of death’, and swiftly overcome the critical stage between pre-competitive results and the market according to previously outlined market deployment timeline (fig 20). In particular, for the SMEs LOEMI and IRIS, which will play a key role in the commercialisation of the new recycling technologies, funding schemes like FTI or SME Instruments will be studied to reach TRL9 after the positive conclusion of this IA. In short: the foundations for achieving the expected impacts of the project have been laid: an innovative research agenda, a resourceful action plan for the contextualisation and exploitation of the expected results, and an excellent conglomerate of actors in the consortium who are in pursuit of a shared common goal.  E. MultiCycle Financial Forecast All industrial partners expect to have significant financial benefits from the exploitation of the project results either by increasing their direct sales, enlarged market share or by savings occurred in materials and energy. In the following paragraphs some basic financial assumptions and initial projections for business planning are presented. The projections concern two revenue streams derived from the exploitation of the recycling lines and of the recycled polymers. Those will be refined and similar financial projections will be done for all industrial partners during the project in the framework of business plan development (Task 9.4 & D9.4).  Available materials to recycle from pi and pc wastes  The first consideration to confirm our process is viable is the availability of suitable materials to recycle (amounts and types). As seen, 50MT of plastics are consumed per year in EU (of which 40% for packaging, 10% for automotive).  In case of packaging, our main focus is on ca. 10 mioT (table 11), but it could be even broader as, although in theory there is no need for MultiCycle process for recycling rigid monolayer packaging unless for specific polymers in low shares, in presence of specific additives that condition secondary use, MultiCycle can avoid downgrading generally observed during mechanical recycling. In case of automotive, our main focus is on 250kT (FRP, table 11), as the estimated share of such material in current cars. It could in fact also grow to the whole plastic parts: despite neat and short fibres reinforced plastics used in car parts are theoretically recyclable mechanically, nowadays it is mainly done for pi waste, and there are a lot of reasons that prevent 
 Fig. 20: Market deployment plan of main MultiCycle KERs (note: a single 
flexible MultiCycle plant could process materials from/for the 2 sectors) 

                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 43 of 117 
recycling ELV wastes whereas pure fractions are needed for mechanical recycling such as: i) difficulties in identifying and separating the parts (in our project we will make advances in the composition monitoring even of black parts), ii) the presence of paints, adhesives, etc., iii) downgrading during mechanical recycling leading to low value SRM. Therefore, we estimate we can tap into 1MioT which is the amount of plastics currently found in ELV12 (plastics use in cars has increased a lot in the last decade, so it will also grow in future ELV wastes to recycle further justifying our solution to arise). Therefore in our 2 target sectors, the MultiCycle route is relevant throughout the used materials and although we focused in the project on technical demonstrations compatible with the greatest challenges (and indeed the fastest growing segments), there is no doubt that when recycling facilities would be available with greater capacity, the process could be extended basically to the rest of the materials wherever relevant. For simplicity reasons as we chose circular reuse, the specific case of our waste generators in the consortium is discussed together with the opportunity for them to reuse as SRM. 
Table 11: Materials shares in our target sectors (dark green: main focus for Multicycle, light 
green compatible with potential advantages discussed above), figures from FARPLAS+ ref 23 
Revenue from the commercialization of recycling lines The Multicycle recycling process requires substantial investments (as do the setup of mechanical recycling plants from scratch, estimated to cost 3M€). CAPEX and OPEX have already been calculated for CreaSolv® applied in different scenarios. For example, CloseWEEE project evaluated recycling of 500 T/a to 5.000 t/a WEEE with ABS and PC/ABS as target polymers from dismantled Monitor computer housing (ICT), ICT and mixture of small domestic appliances. For 500 t/a there was no economic scenario, for 5.000 T/a, 10 €Mio CAPEX (including engineering) and 500- 700 €/t OPEX were calculated depending on the share of target polymers and a larger set of pre-conditions. The costs and economic indicators of the best scenario in economic terms, which was that one in which shredded plastics were used as inputs streams, are reported in in table 12. Based on such figures from another project at advanced TRL, but the greater flexibility we need, we extrapolate for 5.000 T/a capacity input, €10 Mio CAPEX and 500-700 €/T OPEX for multilayers and 700 – 900 €/T in case of composites. That will of course depend on the number of different fractions it should be prepared to recover. FRAUNHOFER will license a group or patents and knowhow to recycling companies and other stakeholders in order to exploit the core step of our recycling process that is based on the CreaSolv® technology. LOEMI and IRIS will build up the recycling lines and their monitoring systems respectively based on contracts concluded with companies having as first clients the MultiCycle partners that will have discounted prices. For example, partners like ELG and GBP have shown interest to host MultiCycle plants to recover specific fractions like GF-PA and PA-PP respectively. ISWA further brings numerous opportunities to license the new process to the affiliates of their national organisations. The licensing fees will be negotiated on a case by case basis (compound model between minimum per year and %). AIMPLAS will further transfer knowhow for the compounding step (not accounted in initial figures below). The following assumptions have been made in the table 13 below: i) Royalties of 1% on the revenues generated by the use of the patented process in the MultiCycle plants, ii) According to above CAPEX, initial commercial price of the production lines with capacity of 5.000 t/year is estimated to be 10 million € with a profit margin of 15%; iii) The revenues from licenses are based on a conservative 200€ profit per ton of recycled polymer as analyzed in the next paragraph “Revenues from sales of recycled materials”, with operation at 90% of full capacity, equal to 4.500t/year; iv) Additional revenue stream in the maintenance and service of the lines; v) Inflation rate of 2%.

The primary target for the first 5 years after the commercialization will be the EU market and secondly the global markets, based on the existing clientele and distribution channels as well as assuming that we will present annual increase of the market share due to the benefits that the new technology will represent for their customers. The pilot plant built in MultiCycle will be maintained as a demo plant in LOEMI and the aim will be to make it portable to facilitate transportation and set up for trials at customers sites for converting prospects thanks to fine case validations. The capacity that can be granted by the 25 recycling lines in place by 2028 would be of 125kT recycled plastic per year, which, when compared with the material listed as our primary focus earlier (10 Mio T of packaging and 1 Mio T of Automotive plastics) is yet a small 1% share recycled but shows a very promising initial market introduction that will warrant a continued growth in the later years. With an expected global market of 300 MioT of recycled plastic in the packaging and automotive sector, further global exploitation (directly or through licenses) will increase significantly the revenues from the project. Revenues from sales/use of recycled materials The following table summarises a market search that has been contacted by the MultiCycle consortium concerning current commercial prices of virgin materials and of SRM. Also the OPEX, machine depreciation (CAPEX divided by 5.000 T and 10 years, leads to amortization cost of €200/T) and expected commercial price per ton of the SRM that will result from the MultiCycle process is projected. 70% of the virgin material costs has been assumed as a general base for the projection of the Multicycle SRM price. Although we expect that the MutliCycle SRM will not suffer any degradation of properties compared to the virgin ones, this ratio of price has been selected on one hand to have competitive price compared to virgin materials (if SRMs and virgin materials have similar price, SRMs will not be attractive) and on the other hand in order for the users of Multicycle technologies to create additional revenues.

From the above table it is evident that the operators of Multicycle pilot lines will have significant revenues especially companies that are in the field of fibers recycling (ELG). Based on the projections concerning the sales of the Multicycle recycling lines presented in the previous paragraph, in 2028 where 39 machines will be operating leading to recycle 125 kT and the same conservative assumption 200€ profit per ton of recycled material (as the shares between the different fraction is not easy to assume upfront), an additional annual profit of €25 Mio will be generated in Europe leading to 250 new jobs in the recycling section of the value chain (not limited to partners).  Finally, additional revenues are expected from the use of the recycled materials by companies downstream of the value chain (and upstream as they will provide then new materials to be recycled) through the commercialisation of different types of semi-finished/processed materials and final products. The use of SRMs in new products will touch the environmental awareness of consumers and will be adopted as a concept by the market. The synopsis of the estimation of increase in turnovers and jobs for all the participants of the Multicycle project, for the first 5 years after the full commercialization of the results is provided in the table 15.  In terms of plastics, ARKEMA will study how a shift to circularity can support selecting recyclable materials from the outset of the value chain. Standard PAs, used as matrix for automotive composites, cannot be easily recycled for now or if they are recycled the final material is significantly downgraded. Bio-based PA of different types (PA10.10, PA11…) as well as PA12 from end of life products will be considered for recycling. As such ARKEMA expect significant growth, for example from the shift from thermosets composites to PA-based ones. AMCOR and SILON will support the compounding of different plastics (PET, PE, PA, PP and PLA when available in sufficient quantity to afford recovery) that will have better properties than the currently available mechanically recycled alternatives. SILON, already active in the recycling value chain using mechanical reprocessing, will further produce technical textile fibres for composites, extending its sales thanks to base SRMs of improved quality. AMCOR will further study SRMs’ convertibility into packaging, especially for the food, personal & home care sectors using innovative processes. 19% Share of demand in Europe flexible packaging in 2016) and its push and pull effect will be fundamental to successfully roll out our recycling process, especially as they have a central role in industry wide initiatives like CEFLEX setting the tone in terms of good practices for packaging. For its customers, packing different goods and putting them on the market, broadly represented in the AB, there will be a positive effect, that could even be reverberated at the consumer level, in relation to decreased packaging waste handling fees (table 16).  To complement the polymer recycling and make the overall process more cost effective, especially when CF are traded above 25€/kg, fibres recovery has been given particular attention. As such, we have included key players for both glass and carbon fibres (BONDL, ELG respectively). They already have experience in recycling fibres and are very keen on exploring the improvements that MultiCycle can provide by avoiding reducing size during recycling and avoiding high energy consumption during their recovery. The combination of recycled plastics and fibres is also of high significance both in terms of environmental and economical sustainability improvements for the participating composite converters and end users companies such as FARPLAS (TIER1) and TOFAS (OEM), who are interested in replacing virgin materials with recycled ones, meet ELV directive, avoid landfilling or incineration taxes (100-200€/tonT or more), but also in substituting metals with composites in structural parts. This is even more needed since TOFAS exports 60% of its total car production to EU where ELV directives are most stringent. BONDL pays 100-200€/Ton for landfilling of their composites waste (they produce 25% process scrap due to part cutting). Same goes for GBP, as PVC, which cannot be taken to incinerators, is present in the light fraction. Eliminating PVC does not compensate the price of the product in the incinerator vs. the cost of landfilling. GBP could recycle PP (but also lower amounts of PA and fibres): taking into account that the r-PP can be priced at € 903/ Ton (table 14) leading to 203€ profit /ton, and that the average production could be 700 Ton / month, GBP expect to be able to generate €1.4M profit per annum (in addition to saving €1.2 M costs of landfilling), leading to an interesting payback of 7 years. The project will confirm it counterbalances added efforts to dismantle additional cars parts. 
2.2.3. How the participants will manage the research data generated and/or collected during the project  Although the exploitation interests will be favoured by default, MultiCycle will participate in the Pilot Open Access Research Data scheme for a selected amount of research data generated by the project. A Data Management Plan has been included as a Deliverable in WP10. Non-confidential results will be widely disseminated in open access media. All publications and selected recycling trials, LCA, and characterisation results will, among other, be considered for this in view of their scientific relevance after an agreed embargo time allowing excluding the commercially sensitive details in case of selected process conditions and successful formulations. All publicly available data will be protected by copyright statements. 2.2.4. Strategy for knowledge management and IPR protection Protection of intellectual property rights (IPR) is of great importance to the participating partners, therefore, the non-confidential results and information on the project to be publicly disseminated will be carefully identified. In order to safeguard commercial and academic interests, dissemination activities will be subject to a confidentiality policy. By default, and building on the consortium members’ experience in collaborative projects, any result of the project will be treated as confidential and will only be disseminated after agreement of the project partners through an approval process following the relevant clauses of the Consortium Agreement. This approach allows us to retain complete control and make sure that no “exploitable” information is disclosed prior to being protected. The RTD participants in the consortium will publish results in peer-reviewed journals, which, if successful, will be an indicator of overall project success, and an important element of dissemination towards industry, standardisation bodies, and policy makers. The industrial partners and market actors will focus on showcasing and disseminating results at conferences and trade fairs, and through other industrial channels such as technical/sectorial magazines, industry network meetings, and the like.  The Dissemination, Training & Communication Manager (Dr John Henderson, BRITEST) will work closely with the project innovators and the IPR & Data Manager (Dr. Pat O’ Sullivan, ILS) as well as the Innovation & Exploitation Manager (Dr. Ioanna Deligkiozi, AXIA) to manage the identification of non-confidential disclosable information that can be published/disseminated. Dr. Henderson will lead the project partners in strategically planning and effectively implementing the dissemination through a dedicated activity on dissemination planning carried out as part of Task T8.2 Dissemination and Stakeholder Engagement. All the measures will be tailored taking into account the project needs, the target groups’ background and the expertise of the project partners. 
The Knowledge Management Strategy (Fig. 21) which will be followed throughout the duration of MultiCycle is heavily interconnected with the exploitation and dissemination activities to pave the way for post-project exploitation. An integrated approach will be followed for the identification, capture, retrieval, distribution, sharing, use and reuse of MultiCycle information and knowledge assets. This will be essential to drive the value and benefits of these information and knowledge assets, and contribute to maximising 
Figure21: Integrated Knowledge Management 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 46 of 117 
the innovation impact of the action. Knowledge management sessions will take place regularly during the project meetings. These sessions will be chaired by the IPR & Data Manager (Dr. Pat O’Sullivan, ILS), attended by all project beneficiaries, and will facilitate the disclosure of the IP and the novelty of the project results. The aim of these sessions is to review: i) Expected results per Work Package (WP), ii) Evaluation of the IP (technology watch/prior art review), iii) Evaluation of protection options (IPR tools evaluation), iv) Third party components included in the project activities including tools and datasets, v) Confidentiality of project results, vi) Expected publications per partner, vii) Exploitation intentions per partner. 
Background IPR and access rights Background is information and knowledge (including inventions, databases, etc.) held by the partners prior to their accession to the EC grant agreement, as well as any intellectual property rights which are needed for carrying out the project or for using results held by the consortium, or indeed by third parties. We have defined the current status of Background at proposal preparation stage (Table 13). This will be revised again and included in the Consortium Agreement prior to signature of the Grant Agreement. Access to Background that is, or will be found to be, necessary for the implementation of the project will be granted royalty free and background needed to use the results of the project will be provided either royalty free or under fair and reasonable conditions.

Results definition, protection, ownership and access rights  Selected results from the project were previously listed in Table 9 along with the expected scenarios of results ownership. This table will be updated during project implementation to ensure we account for all project results in line with developments. The project results will be continually assessed in terms of novelty, patentability and protection (with a target >2 new filed patents) through Task 9.3, as part of an effective knowledge management strategy. IP protection (patents) will be prioritised based on MultiCycle’s anticipated commercial roadmap, in turn providing a valuable commercial incentive to the commercialising actors as well as both a competitive and strategic go-to-market advantage that will also set up opportunities for Venture Capital and/or Angel Investor funding (where investor confidence is typically increased when patent protection is confirmed). By default, results will be owned by the partner generating them. To avoid doubt or confusion over ownership, a protocol of procedures will be defined, which will be followed by each partner to clearly show the development of the generation of knowledge or results. In case a result is jointly generated (i.e. where the separate parts of some result are attributed to different participants), it will be jointly owned. Consequently, Joint Ownership Agreements will regulate the 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 47 of 117 
allocation and the terms of exercising this joint ownership(s). However, an overriding principle will be that the participating industry partners, especially SMEs, are always provided with the access that they need to the results to exploit them in keeping with the needs and strategy of their business. As such, access rights to results for implementing the project will be granted on a royalty-free basis. Access rights for use purposes will be granted either royalty-free, or on fair and reasonable conditions (these conditions will be established in Exploitation Agreements that will be negotiated between the requester and granter of the access rights). First drafts of Exploitation Agreements will be discussed by M18, and final versions will be signed at M36 and annexed to D9.4. 2.2.5. Open access to peer-reviewed scientific publications MultiCycle partners will provide peers and end-users with free of charge online access to the project peer-reviewed scientific research articles. Partners can follow two routes towards open access (1) by self-archiving their journal articles in an open access repository, also known as 'green' open access, or (2) by publishing in an open access journal, known as 'gold' open access. Whereas fees to cover publishing in gold open access journals have been budgeted, in case significantly greater impact factors are then accessible, MultiCycle will privilege green open access, whereby the partners will publish in peer-reviewed scientific publications and then self-archive a version in repositories (using only those indexed in specialised search engines like Scopus). The project website will complementarily house the ‘MultiCycle Green Channel’ for open access to the peer-reviewed publications of the project. In relation to expected publications from the project, these include but are not limited to: results from LCA, usability of SRMs, food safety analysis, use of the recycled polymers in packaging laminates, dual recovery of CF and polymer matrix, process for composite and packaging recycling, etc. b) Communication activities Effective communication activities are important for promoting our action and outputs to our target stakeholders in order to generate awareness and positive perceptions of the MultiCycle SPIRE innovation action, and positive engagement with its results. To successfully connect, engage, cooperate with, and acquire positive support from relevant stakeholders, including the public at large, a strategic plan will be implemented in Task 8.2, building on the preliminary outline presented in Table 17. The overall communications campaign, covering the whole project duration, will be led by the Training, Communication & Dissemination Manager, Dr. John Henderson (BRITEST), heavily supported by V2020, who will take advantage of established extensive network and communications capacity to ensure that benefits and impacts of the project are effectively conveyed to key stakeholders including industrialists -especially SPIRE community-, policy makers, and the general public. Our aim is to i) generate understanding of what MultiCycle seeks to achieve, ii) stimulate interest and support, and iii) motivate the collaboration of actors across the value chain through to public consumers of plastic-based and packaged goods in order to boost the transition to the circular economy in EU. Our communications strategy leverages one-way, bilateral, and multilateral channels. Following a preliminary stakeholder analysis (to be further developed in Task 8.2), the target audiences of MultiCycle communication activities comprise polymers manufacturers and compounders, composites and packaging converters, polymer and composite recyclers, food and other packaging end users and retailers, carbon and glass fibres producers, textile producers, engineering companies, consumers, associations, public authorities, standardisation and safety agencies/organisations and scientific communities.  Particular efforts will be made to engage with and raise awareness amongst the general public, whereby we will take the opportunity to address consumers’ concerns by informing the public about issues (such as our current strong dependence on fossil-based conventional plastics and composites, lack of design for reuse and consequent single use cycles, and deficient end of life options like land filling and downcycling), but also to positively frame existing and emerging possibilities and provide a sense of agency – that is that individuals can make positive choices that will make a difference, and thereby enhance their lifestyle and society. In this way, individuals become aware actors motivated to make responsible choices about, for example, using recyclable materials, collecting adequately used packaging, and thus exerting pressure for change on major manufacturing value chains (e.g. optimal handling of ELV materials in the automotive sector). The awareness raising actions carried out in MultiCycle will use optimal transparency and appropriate language, enabling EU consumers to make informed purchasing and consumption decisions, for example bolstering the message that recycled materials can display equal performance to virgin ones. A set of key messages will be developed specifically to popularise complex subjects and to bring the issue closer to people’s lives by making it more understandable. These will be rolled out through various channels and tools, with the following approach: 1. ‘Push’ communication (graphic identity, newsletters, press releases, promotional materials, website gadgets, etc.) and 2. ‘Pull’ communication and interaction (social media, liaison with other initiatives and projects including community groups, face to face events, joint communication, etc.). The fact that our demonstrators are broadly focused on consumer applications like packaging and automotive will assist with maximising the awareness of the positive impact of the Circular Economy, and indeed of the SPIRE programme, on everyday lives. The use of recycled plastics needs to be properly explained and marketed. We will use a strong green branding providing information in digital factsheets and infographics e.g. about: recycling, green house gases emissions saving, etc. Our messages on safety, environmental protection, circular sourcing and recyclability are extremely consistent with the green policy of our end users and a majour community of companies and retailers, which will be leveraged.

3.1 Work plan — Work packages, deliverables  Brief presentation of the overall structure of the work plan A competitive work plan has been devised in order to deliver on the objectives of the MultiCycle project providing recycling and reuse solutions for a number of packaging and composites especially used in automotive and packaging applications. This will provide new business opportunities, preserve our resources and environment, help the European Process Industries to meet sustainability KPIs, but also meet the expectations of green-minded consumers. The project objectives (from Section 1.1) have been used as the starting point for planning the work that needs to be implemented in the work plan. All the actions that are required to fulfil the project objectives have been collectively analysed by the interdisciplinary consortium. It was agreed how best to distribute the workload among the various experts in keeping with their relevant fields of knowledge, capacity, infrastructure, resources and in the context of involved supply and value chains, including waste management aspects. This bottom-up planning process has greatly facilitated the task of arriving at a clear, comprehensive and realistic work plan (see Gantt Chart and Pert Diagram). The work plan is split into 10 WPs logically distributed along the value chain, each with clear tasks that will enable the project’s objectives to be fulfilled through a coherent structure that will also facilitate project. To ensure the clear focus towards the target application of this Innovation Action building upon past successful research, most WPs, except WP1-2-3 dedicated to initial review of state of the art, specifications and process development, are led by industrial partners (with a key RTD providing support in the validation WPs, respectively AIMPLAS for compounding, FRAUNHOFER for packaging as well as recycling process upscaling and CTB for composites, fibres and textile in WP4-6). The first phase (WP1) deals with industry-led specifications definition to guide the following innovation work to match the market needs. The second phase (WP2-3) deals with the pilot scale development and upscaling of the suitable recycling process and formulations from secondary materials to meet the properties for each application. The third phase (WP4-6) deals with the demonstration of the processes and of the materials for different end uses. In a fourth transversal stage (WP7), we will cover the environmental and economic aspect of the developed processes and products with a toll gate at midterm to provide inputs to transition from phase 2 to phase 3. To complement the first 7 WPs linked with technical development and demonstration activities, in the last phase dedicated to project and innovation management, transversal activities like value chain study, knowledge management, exploitation, business planning and dissemination will be undertaken to prepare the market uptake and maximise the project impact. The work plan is clearly linked to the objectives (and related KPIs used as milestones) set out in Section 1.1 and the consortium is confident that this work plan is robust to ensure that the project’s technical and innovation goals are fulfilled. The development and demonstration work is encased in a comprehensive risk assessment and management strategy, sound communication and training to heighten the impact of the knowledge generated, as well as knowledge management to pave the way for planning and implementing the project’s exploitation and dissemination pathways, and robust project coordination and ISO certified project management procedures. The WPs and tasks form a complex inter-weaved matrix of actions, many interdependent, with very close interaction between the WP and task leaders, which is essential for the delivery of the full suite of outputs. During the work planning stage, consideration has been given to logistical matters, such as the time needed for ordering parts/components, the time needed for shipping of prototypes from development to test sites, installation times, the ‘what if’ scenarios of parts not arriving, installation work throwing up challenges, delays in samples, delays in necessary inputs/results from feed in tasks or subtasks, etc. The link between WPs in terms of needed output material/sample quantity required by next activities were also accounted for. As this is a highly experienced development team, and in particular with many members of the consortium experienced in applied R&I under tight timeframes, as well as experience in integration work at industrial sites, the work planning has benefited from this ‘on the ground’ pragmatic experience, especially in terms of consideration of interdependencies and timings. The potential risks have been identified and contingency plans proposed, which are discussed in Section 3.1 under “Critical risks and mitigation measures”. Importantly, the collective planning of the work plan has achieved the ‘buy-in’ of all the project participants and an early understanding of the importance of a team effort, team planning and active communication among WP and task leaders. Moreover, the one-page Gantt itself (which includes all the deliverables and milestones) will be a very effective tool for high level control and obtaining a “helicopter overview” as part of effective project management during the project.

3.2 Management structure, milestones and procedures  Organisational structure and decision-making  The project will be managed by the experienced Project Coordinator (PC), IRIS. They follow proven project management procedures, which adhere to ISO 9001:2008 certified processes and UNE-EN-ISO 166002: Management Systems for R&D&I. The organisational structure (Fig. 27) consists of the Project Coordinator (the interface with the EC), who is supported by a Project Steering Committee in the strategic management of the project. The Coordinator will ensure the timely and accurate completion of the questionnaires sent by A.SPIRE for the annual progress monitoring report of the SPIRE cPPP. At executive management level, the PC is supported in the implementation of the work by a Technical Committee on the one hand and an Innovation Committee on the other, the roles and decision-making capacity of which are discussed below. As opposed to a monopoly role of the PC, this structure distributes the governance between various partners according to their background to allow a good balance in decision and management of different aspects. An Advisory Board (AB) is also included, which consists of a voluntary group of industry actors and stakeholders who will engage with the project at different points along the work plan. This body has no voting rights and a Confidentiality Agreement will be in place with all AB members. Project coordinator  The Project Coordinator (Dr. Ana Maria Lopez, formerly Dr. Elodie Bugnicourt from IRIS) is responsible for the day to day running of the project, and for ensuring that the work programme adheres to its schedule, fulfils its objectives, and that the project meets the needs and expectations of all the partners. She will be active in steering the evolution of the project by closely monitoring the work performed by each partner. She will assume responsibility for ensuring that the project management procedures are correctly implemented and that all deadlines and obligations are met. She will also act as the communication and administrative interface with the EC, to carry out the reporting and overall administrative management of the project. She will also in many instances act as the public face for the project e.g. in networking events between EU projects or when the project receives enquiries by external agents like press or policy makers. As this is also a leadership role, it requires a profile that is sufficiently experienced and capacitated, not only to supervise the execution of the work programme and delegation of the technical and innovation implementation, but also one that possesses strong communication skills, can foment team-building, motivate the team and encourage proactive participation, facilitate collective problem solving and can show leadership and direction in the taking of timely and appropriate corrective actions where necessary.  As an organisation, IRIS has demonstrable experience, the available technical and administrative resources (with an 
 
Fig. 27: Overview of MultiCycle Management Structure 
 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 53 of 117 
experienced Project Management Office that will provide support to the Project Manager in administrative, financial and contractual management tasks), linguistic and communications skills to successfully perform project management coordination tasks. In terms of the competence of Dr. Bugnicourt to successfully execute this role, she brings a wealth of project management experience from both private contract research and funded EC projects. In FP7, she leveraged her industry knowledge and expertise to the coordination two  “Research for SMEs” projects: DURAWOOD (Grant Agreement No. 232296); and, OPTIJECT (FP7 GA nº 262623), as well as an NMP project OliPHA (FP7 GA nº280604): Among others, she was also technical manager and heavily involved in the General Steering Committee for the “Research for SME Associations” project, WHEYLAYER (Grant Agreement No. 218340-2) and follow up Demonstration Activity project WHEYLAYER2 (FP7 GA nº 315743). In H2020, she is now managing an NMP-Pilots project called OPTINANOPRO (Grant Agreement No. 686116) that is developing solutions for the processing and control of novel nanomaterials in packaging, automotive and solar panel processing lines. She is very familiar with all the modalities and administrative procedures involved in the management of EC research projects, from the grant preparation process, preparing and maintaining the Consortium Agreement, monitoring work, reporting requirements, the use of tools for communication with the EC, etc. Prior to this, she has successfully managed R&D projects in the chemical industry, and in particular in the development of nanocomposites as well as FRP and bio-sourced resins. She has shown herself to be a motivational leader, a fair and intelligent facilitator, with a sound understanding not only of materials science but also of process and associated industries. In addition to having been involved in some of the projects feeding into MultiCycle, Ana Maria is driven by a genuine green awareness, is passionately involved in sustainable processes for 10+ years, and she worked also as project coordinator previously giving her invaluable experience in relation to Project Management. Project Steering Committee To ensure that the project is implemented according to the needs and requirements of all the project partners, and to empower each partner organisation with a role in the strategic management of the project, a Project Steering Committee (PSC) is established. It consists of a key representative of each of the beneficiaries. The PSC will meet every 6 months (or for remote extraordinary meetings convoked by the Project Coordinator in case of urgent matters to be solved in between) and will be facilitated through the chair of the Project Coordinator. Through this Committee the partners will closely monitor and control the work performed, following the principles of “quality, time, cost”. The Project Milestones (Table 3.2 a) will be used as a valuable tool for evaluating critical performance and determining whether or not the project is on schedule. In terms of decision making mechanisms, when strategic decisions need to be taken, such as action to solve conflicts, action to mitigate critical risks to project implementation, action to manage changes in the work plan, decisions in relation to IPR and exploitation, etc., it will be the responsibility of the Project Coordinator to seek consensus with the PCS, whereby each beneficiary will be assigned one vote. Decisions will be taken by simple majority. In the event of a tie situation, the Coordinator will take the final decision. Each representative of the PSC will be responsible for mobilising and coordinating the active and coherent participation of the other personnel from their organisation who are involved in the project team, and they will also ensure that reporting and cost statements from their organisation are submitted on time and correctly.  Technical Committee  The Technical Committee (TC) is established to take care of scientific and technical (S&T) issues and for supporting the Project Coordinator in ensuring that the S&T objectives and activities of the project are successfully accomplished in a timely manner, taking into account the interdisciplinary approach and the interdependence between the technical tasks of the work plan. The TC is composed of the WP and task contributors in WP1-7 who are further gathered into 2 Working Groups (WGs) allowing the required interactions across the WPs developing the key individual technical results. The TC is chaired by the Technical Manager, who will preside over the WP leaders and WGs: Dr. Martin Schlummer, a Senior Researcher from FRAUNHOFER IVV, whom research specialises in the field of plastic recycling and who has already occupied that role in numerous EU-projects. FRAUNHOFER is optimally suited for supporting this role as they cover the not only the recycling proprietary knowledge at the basis of the project but also expertise in plastic conversion and in the packaging sector as key application for the project. Each WG is led an expert from the respective thematic and will consist of the contributing partners (note partners involved in market aspects (AXIA, V2020, BRITEST, ILS) will work with any of the WG when relevant): • WG1: packaging (leader: AMCOR, participants: FRAUNHOFER, AIMPLAS, SILON, IRIS, VERTECH, ISWA) • WG2: composites (leader: TOFAS, participants: BONDL, ELG, FARPLAS, FRAUNHOFER, CTB, SILON, BFP, ARKEMA, VERTECH, ISWA) The appointment of WGs will greatly facilitate the implementation of the work, especially in view of the ambitious, highly interdisciplinary nature of the activities involved. Whereas the WP leaders, who will themselves be fuelled by their task contributors and their teams, will assume responsibility for the implementation of the work towards the fulfilment of the associated Project Objectives (and milestones related to the WPs), each WG will ensure the consistency (selection of processes suitable for later scale up at end users) and compatibility (e.g. amount of materials needed for different purposes of validation, etc.) between WPs within their thematic through the proper engagement of the partners and entrusting to the WPs leaders. The WG leaders will act as facilitators for the integration of the transversal knowledge without losing the 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 54 of 117 
benefit from synergies between the recycling/conversion processes involved in the 2 applications thanks to the common partners involved. The respective WG leaders will ensure the seamless integration of the results of the group by convoking meetings on a regular basis with the implementation teams (virtually or on-site meetings will be scheduled as appropriate). It is expected that the WG structure will increase the potential for early identification of inter-WP scientific or technical risks and WP deviations level that could impact other activities in their thematic to enable effective and timely management (using the procedures outlined in task 10.2). Each WP leader will report on the status and progress of the work involved in their WP to the Project Coordinator in monthly remote and quarterly presential meetings of the Technical Committee (chaired by the Technical Manager). While the TC will have the mandate to take decisions that relate to the implementation of the workplan, any decisions that could affect the scope of the work, its budget, or have a strategic impact on the project will be escalated via the Project Coordinator to the PSC for voting and decision taking.    Innovation Committee  The Innovation Committee (IC) is responsible for identifying and managing innovation from the project. A key goal will be to ensure that the project results are effectively protected and exploited to the full for future industrial growth and competitiveness. The IC consists of a qualified representative from each beneficiary.  This Committee assumes the important role of Innovation Management in the project. Within this context, the IC committee will manage the implementation of the innovation cycle, and the feedback linkages to the development work. It will evaluate the knowledge as it is generated in line with its market impacts. It will also ensure that the project results are effectively protected and exploited to the full for future industrial growth and competitiveness. Key strategic issues, such as IPR protection mechanisms, IPR sharing and routes to exploitation will be discussed extensively during meetings the IC. The IC will also ensure that Business and Exploitation Plans are further developed based on the draft plans from Section 2.2.  The IC will be chaired by the Innovation & Exploitation Manager, Dr. Ioanna Deligkiozi (AXIA) who will lead the partners in effective management of the innovation cycle of the project. She will also act as a driver for the industrial partners in defining the post-project concerted value chain commercial strategy. She has, for this role, the perfect balance between technical (as Chemical Engineer by formation) and market knowledge of the issues related with the introduction of new technologies on the market. She will be supported by Dr. John Henderson (BRITEST) will also act as Training, Communication & Dissemination Manager–His native English, polymer scientist education, strong track record in process improvement, training, facilitation and leading dissemination and deployment programmes in collaborative environments will strengthen his capacity for accurate communication fundamental for the role. This role is very aligned with the main mission of BRITEST in the transfer of knowledge to the process industry. They will be supported by V2020 in terms of Circular Plastic Helix creation and ISWA in terms of outreach towards policy makers. John will be responsible for leading the consortium in further planning and implementing the project Communication and Dissemination activities, as well as ensuring the project reaches out to different audiences in the most effective way, and will also ensure the confidentiality of the results is safeguarded until the IP is protected. An IPR & Data Manager- Dr. Pat O’Sullivan of ILS- will also be appointed. He will be responsible for assisting the partners in identifying and managing innovation from the project, advising on the scope and potential of the innovations arising from the project, and paying attention to discerning IP at regular checkpoints. He will also mobilise the rich expertise of other members of the consortium in IPR management and in successful commercialization and spin-outs. Pat will also supervise data management, ensuring a systematic curation, an important aspect to ensure no knowledge slips through the net. Dr. O’Sullivan has successfully filed over 500 patents across his career, dozens of which were filed in joint names with collaborating partners on research projects and subsequently commercialised, and is therefore intimately familiar with the process for handing and managing IP, Patent Filings, Outside Council and Office Actions from Patent Offices. The IC will meet regularly (at least every 6 months) and pay attention to discerning IP at regular checkpoints. The Innovation & Exploitation Manager supported by the IPR & Data manager will have holistic view of the innovations arising out of the project, and will advise individual partners on IPR and funding strategy, with the aim of maximizing the commercial potential of the innovations created. IP ownership will belong by default to the partner’s creating it, and will be subject to terms and conditions outlined in the Consortium Agreement. The Innovation & Exploitation Manager will update the Project Coordinator of the innovations arising, and recommendations for next steps on how best to handle it. In relation to decision making, the IC will decide on all matters with regard to IPR and exploitation, whereby each beneficiary can decide how best to protect and own its own IP, and joint IP will be decided by the beneficiaries involved, with each beneficiary in this case being assigned a vote (and decisions being taken by simple majority). The IC will have the ability to take decisions that relate to the implementation of the WP9, but any strategic decision such as those in relation to IPRs that affect the consortium, etc. will be raised to the PSC for voting. Moreover, in meetings of the PSC, the Innovation & Exploitation Manager on behalf of the IC will report on the status of Innovation Management Activities.  Appropriateness to the complexity and scale of the project This organisational structure is considered appropriate for the management of this project, in view of its size, multidisciplinary nature and its S&T scope and ambition, and its expected innovation impacts. With key roles and responsibilities, each body 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 55 of 117 
within the structure ensures that there is dedicated and qualified management and skills-sets mobilised at the different levels, as opposed to a centralised, one-dimensional management approach, which would be highly unlikely to deliver the necessary versatility. To this end, there is an integral approach to the management of the project, ensuring:   effective management of the day-to-day running of the project & its resources, appropriate interfacing with the EC (Project Coordinator)  all participants have a stake in the strategic management of the project (Project Steering Committee)  effective management of the execution of the interdisciplinary work plan and its ability to deliver impact, whereby two executive-level Committees have been planned- the Technical Committee will be responsible for ensuring the project achieves its research objectives through sound management of the overall S&T work and it potential risks; the Innovation Committee will be responsible for effective dissemination and management of the knowledge and data generated, Intellectual Property Rights (IPRs), as well as the other innovation-related activities required to deliver market exploitable results and to map out the route for further research in order to build on the project results. Innovation management  A key objective of publicly-funded research is that it should lead to the exploitation of results, which goes one step further than the mere production and dissemination of new scientific knowledge85. As such, our project envisages an Innovation Committee and an Innovation & Exploitation Manager in its organisation structure (as described above) and we also have a dedicated Task 9.1 for Innovation Management, which will be closely linked with the IP and Knowledge Management work in Task 9.3. Our Innovation Manager, Dr. Ioanna Deligkiozi from AXIA, has the necessary entrepreneurial spirit to foment the conditions for an innovation eco-system to flourish. She has a deep business understanding, along with a strong technical background for ensuring creative ideas are anchored to the required market, regulatory and performance requirements. In addition to IP protection, our Innovation Management activities will pivot around the implementation of an innovation cycle, which is a vital component for ensuring success in market-oriented research projects. Close interaction with the Technical Committee will be promoted to facilitate co-creation and fluid interaction between the development teams and our industry end-users. Open brainstorming, idea management, and the Quality Function Deployment (QFD) method employed at the project outset in WP1 will contribute to a deep understanding of the market needs, industry constraints and regulatory/standards framework. Thereafter, and in particular through WP2-3 where the recycling and reprocessing processes will be upscaled to produce the new materials in sufficient amounts in WP4 for WP5-6 whereby validation for 2 sectors end users case studies will be performed. The market strive will accompany the entire work plan and the development teams will be guided by feedback loops from fellow developers, end-users and market insights to fuel an innovation cycle that leads to exploitable project results for a successful project outcome in terms of innovation impacts. In addition to the market drive of the technical development, we are in fact embedding our innovation strategy in a quadruple innovation helix model by contextualizing it within number of inputs from/ and outcome to the ‘natural environments of society’ as well as ‘media-based and culture-based public’ and ‘civil society’ by involving interactions with governmental/policy makers as well as stakeholders including consumers again contributing to a multiplying effect for our innovation potential and impact (fig. 28). Our V2020 Helix also provide an open innovation environment to generate new collaborations. Critical risks and mitigation measures Risks can be categorised as follows; technology related risks, market related risks and transversal risks (environment related risks and organisation related risks)86. Technical risks will be managed by the Technical Committee through its thematic working groups. Market-related risks and environment related risks will be closely analysed by the Innovation Committee, and organisational-related risks will be managed by the Project Coordinator supported by the Project Steering Committee and will be embedded in the close monitoring of the evolution of the work. Table 3.2b shows the main risks identified to date, their probability of occurrence, the severity of the problem (impact), the actions to mitigate the risk and foreseen contingency actions. Through Task 10.2.1, a continuous process of risk identification and management (of these risks below and any newly identified risks) will be implemented based upon the principles and guidelines of the FERMA (ISO 31000 related) Standard87. Agreement sought for risk-related contingency actions from the TC (in relation to technical risks) and the IC (in relation to market- and environment-related risks) will be taken by the PSC.  As will be further defined in the Consortium Agreement, in case of a significant unexpected risk requiring a quick decision,

In addition, as seen, a decision procedure has been defined within each committee, where each member has a vote. To avoid the case where the corrective/decided action is blocked due to disagreements between the different committees, all transversal decisions as well as decisions of strategic importance to the Project will be the mandate of the PSC, acting above the other committees. 3.3 Consortium as a whole  Bringing together public and private organisations across Europe to perform collaborative research and development is key for the interdisciplinary approach often needed for Circular Economy based innovation linked to highly diverse markets. The MultiCycle consortium is made up of 19 partners from 10 countries: 7 EU countries (Belgium, Spain, Germany, France, Czech Republic, Greece, UK), 2 associated countries (Turkey, Switzerland, involving partners with major European sales to safeguard we maintain our core impacts in EU, e.g. TOFAS, partly owned by FIAT, (and its TIER1 FARPLAS) work mainly for European OEMs who are in turn responsible for ELVs), as well as Austria-based international associations with significant policy outreach capacity. As such we have an integrative consortium covering a broad geography and from small Eastern EU-firms to world leading blue-chip Global companies. Our team constitutes a pan-European eco-system that unites the necessary infrastructure, knowledge, expertise, services and industry value chain to succeed in delivering on the project’s objectives to realise the expected impacts of the topic CE-SPIRE10-2018- Efficient recycling processes for plastic containing materials. As illustrated in fig. 16, the consortium brings together comprehensive interdisciplinary pockets of knowledge. There is solid scientific presence covering all relevant fields (AIMPLAS, FRAUNHOFER, CTB), combined with strong industrial and commercial involvement (7 SMEs, 1 AGs and 8 large enterprises) to ensure that the value chain of commercial actors can progress swiftly towards the introduction of new viable solutions for the recycling of plastic based multi-materials. To this end, RTOs working closely with technology and engineering companies (LOEMI, IRIS), polymer and fibres producers/recyclers (ARKEMA, BONDL, ELG), compounder specialised in recycled plastics (SILON), packaging manufacturer (AMCOR), composite converter (BONDL, FARPLAS, TOFAS), service providing SMEs in terms of TEA, LCC and LCA (VERTECH) as well as global waste management association ISWA. To strengthen impact, complementary partners have been included in terms of transversal activities and to ensure a business-driven focus to the project: innovation, market and supply chain study and exploitation planning (AIXA), IPR & Data management (ILS), training (BRITEST), Dissemination (V2020). In short, this is a complete storyboard of actors. Importantly the consortium will draw on the voluntary and objective advice, expertise and support of an external Advisory Board, in order to complement the consortium’s industrial and technical insight in relevant fields. AB members include FPE, CEFLEX, EuCIA, EUBP, PACKCLUSTER, IVLV, ZLV, ANARPLA, ARCTIC, DIAD, AEROCIRCULAR. Each will provide invaluable insights into different applications to be recycled or that could use SRM, and could act as strategic adopters obtaining a license for the developed technologies.  As is fitting for an IA, this is an industry-oriented project. It is coordinated by the highly experienced technology SME, IRIS, and a number of the WP leaders are also from industry. The industry participants have been instrumental in helping to shape this project, and MultiCycle is considered a highly strategic for assisting them in gaining a competitive edge and in being a pioneering group in driving forward Circular Economy-enabled innovations in the field of plastics. Each industry participant will be able to grow their businesses and improve their competitiveness in line with their position on the value chain. Fig. 29, where partners are clustered according to their positioning on the supply and value chain, shows how each partner is relevant and complementary.  Finally, in relation to the gender dimension of our consortium, our team of researchers, technologists, industrialists, business experts, and policy influencers has a good participation of females. A number of the key management and decision-making positions within the project organisational structure have been assigned to highly qualified women, not least the roles of the Project Coordinator (Dr. Ana Maria Lopez and formerly Dr. Elodie Bugnicourt) and Innovation & Exploitation

3.4 Resources to be committed The project budget has been built using a bottom-up methodology. There is a logical budget distribution among the partners and each has mobilised the necessary staff/person months (Table 3.4a) and infrastructure to get the job done in a competitive timeframe. The necessary personnel resources reflect the realistic efforts required to deliver the project objectives, and have been costed up based on actual costs for staff for each partner organisation (in line with average staff costs within the sectors and reflected the differences in cost of living between the different participating EU regions). Equipment costs included in the project are for depreciation only. A reasonable budget has been allocated to mobility of developers and industrialists between sites, which is important for knowledge exchange and building excellence among multidisciplinary and international teams, as well as for practical on-site testings across partners as needed. Minimal subcontracting costs have been budgeted as the consortium has the internal resources and knowledge to perform the work.  The other goods and services (OG&S) that need to be contracted have also been included (and a breakdown of these costs are provided in Table 3.4b- for those partners whose Other costs exceed 15% of personnel costs). In line with the piloting and demonstration activities in this IA project, material costs have been budgeted to reach large prototype series of the target demonstrators for each sector. It should be further stressed that in the range of 1 M€ is allocated in direct costs for the MultiCycle pilot plant (included in the budgets of LOEMI and IRIS) that will be used for pre-competitive demonstration of the feasibility of the multi-material recycling and will be further upgraded to a portable version to facilitate ease of transportation for facilitating post project exploitation. It is noteworthy that the cost of this pilot plant, in addition to the critical and significant role of FRAUNHOFER in supporting the industrial implementation of its proprietary process, as well as their role related to packaging technology, along with the presence of 2 more German partners, accounts for the significant budget share directed to Germany. The SMEs and industry partners occupy a sufficient portion of the budget (49% of the EC contribution goes to SMEs- 2 of which non-profit, 20% to large, 2.5% to the involved AG and 28.5% to RTDs), ensuring a practical research-industry partnership for high competitive impact. There is an appropriate budget allocated to communication, dissemination, IP and knowledge management and business planning activities for impact. In relation to the EC Contribution, SMEs and industry partners will finance 30% their budgets- which each is readily committed to doing given the strategic nature of the project for their businesses and the expected growth potential generated. As such, the mobilisation of private co-funding heightens the impact for the EC of this ambitious project. Considering that there are 19 partners in the project, and a competitive 36-month work plan that will output knowledge, high TRL processes and pilot plant for recycling plastic based multi-materials, in addition to packaging and composite demonstrators from SRM with high transferability to other industries, a requested EC Contribution below 7.7M€ is very good value for high impact.

   4.1. Participants (applicants)  Participant no. 1 Name (short name)/ Country Innovacio i Recerca Industrial i Sostenible S.L. (IRIS) / Spain Description of the legal entity  IRIS (www.iris-eng.com) is an advanced engineering company that was established in Barcelona in 2007 and specialises in the manufacture and integration of Process Analytical Technology (PAT)-based real-time quality monitoring solutions for the process industries and IoT systems for smart manufacturing, predominantly targeted at the food, pharmaceutical and chemical industries. In particular, our product line comprises proprietary state-of-the-art IR analysers for at-line and in-line process monitoring.  On the one hand our solutions are based on the latest developments in photonics-in the fields of MEMs Near Infrared Spectroscopy, Hyperspectral Imaging, and Raman. On the other hand, we leverage ICTs, whereby we apply Machine learning, Artificial Intelligence and Data Mining techniques for predictive and active control, as well as for decision support

820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 60 of 117 
systems. In addition to photonics-based analysers, we also work with IoT sensors for enabling equipment and machinery for smart manufacturing and transitioning industry to the Industry4.0 paradigm. Our solutions are cloud-based for mobility, remote monitoring and multi-site applications.  Our Innovation Division which is governed by an Innovation Committee comprising Senior Management, and an Innovation Unit for managing and executing the companies innovation roadmap, has 3 core strategic lines, for shaping the Factory of the Future: 1. New optical photonics-based technologies and PAT systems for production process optimisation 2. ICTs (Artificial intelligence / machine learning, IOT & cloud platforms) 3. Circular and Bio-based Economy: digitalization and waste valorisation In addition to running an internal R&D programme, as well as carrying our various joint private projects with industry partners, our Innovation Division is highly experienced in working in collaborative R&D projects in H2020 (both as coordinators and partners), and principally in the SPIRE, FoF, and BBI programmes. The company was established in 2007 and currently employs 60+ staff with a turnover of € 5 M. It is equipped with engineering workshops (3), food testing and wet chemistry laboratory, optics labs (2), and an electronics/telecommunications lab. IRIS is ISO 9001:2008 certified and also is certified under UNE-EN-ISO 166002: Management Systems R&D&i. We are also certified by the local (Catalonian) government as a provider of technological R&D services (Tecnio Certification). IRIS is a member of the following sectorial networks: SPIRE, SPIE and SECPHO, in addition to regional industrial collectives such as Smart Space (association of companies and organisations that specialise in the ‘smartification’ of industry).

Related services: Chemiometrics model development and training A list of up to 5 relevant previous projects or activities, connected to the subject of this project 1. Hypera®-Hyperspectral Imaging (HSI) System for on-line foreign body detection and chemical composition analysis (H2020- SMEInst, Grant Agreement no: 726572, 2016-2018). IRIS is further developing its inline HSI monitoring product line and validating it in the food industry. To date the system has been applied commercially for inline pizza quality assessment (topping distribution and absence of plastic flakes). Using HSI, not only the shape or position but also the chemical composition and hidden matter can be unveiled. Using Artificial Intelligence algorithms (chemometrics), we convert the monitored outputs into meaningful information much beyond that can be obtained with conventional vision systems. Our HSI system prevents out-of-spec processes or product recalls.  2. ProPAT- Robust and affordable process control technologies for improving standards and optimising industrial operations (H2020- SPIRE, Grant Agreement no: 637232, 2014-2018). IRIS is leading the development of a global PAT control platform integrating novel low cost smart sensors, such as MEMS-NIR spectroscopic and granulometric analysers, which together with chemometric tools and predictive software, is being validated to optimise the performance of batch and continuous processing industries. As initial target applications, the milling of minerals, ceramics, metals, mixing and granulation of pharma products and polymerization of resins are optimized both in terms of process efficiency, and output quality. 3. ShareBox- Secure Management Platform for Shared Process Resources, Grant Agreement no: 680843, Funding programme: H2020-SPIRE-06-2015. IRIS is developing the central cloud platform that integrates a number of smart modules for supporting and optimising synergies in the use of shared resources and waste valorisation between companies. Based on agent based modelling (ABM) approach, the supply-demand matching brings Industrial Symbiosis into the Industry4.0 paradigm, and allows the shift of industrial users towards the circular economy. 4. WHEYLAYER- Whey protein-coated plastic films to replace expensive polymers and increase recyclability. (Grant agreement no.: 218340-2 Completed). Followed by WHEYLAYER2- Barrier biopolymers for sustainable packaging (Grant Agreement no: 315743 FP7, R4SME, Demonstration action Completed). IRIS participated in the process upscaling and optimisation, among other via simulation and monitoring, for multilayer films with improved recyclability by design. 5. OPTIJECT- A novel spectroscopic instrument for in-line monitoring during injection moulding (FP7, R4SMEs, Grant agreement no.: 262623, 2010-2012). IRIS has implemented NIR and UV-VIS spectroscopy-based in line monitoring systems for the polymer melt stream within the nozzle of injection machines. In particular, IRIS delivered probes able to withstand outstandingly high pressure and temperatures up to 350ºC. The system was demonstrated to provide information normally only available to plastic converters via laborious off line QC tests like material humidity down to 
                         CE-SPIRE-10-2018  
820695- MultiCycle- Part B   Page 64 of 117 
ppm level or polymer degradation, as well as to allow optimising colour and colourant concentration in real time. When coupled with case based reasoning models, it allows decision support for real time process optimization thereby maximizing yield and minimizing the scrap generated.
